Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Interest From Abroad Has Returned --- Investors Return to the U.S. As...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Interest From Abroad Has
Returned --- Investors Return to the U.S. As Economy Shows Strength, Rate Hikes Seem Less Likely
By Peter A. McKay
629 words
5 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
GLOBAL INVESTORS HAVE gotten more aggressive over the last month, bidding up assets often used as
bets on rapid growth and shunning traditional havens except for gold.
Their resolve has been bolstered by generally encouraging data about the U.S. economy and the hoped-for
end next year of the Federal Reserve's spate of interest-rate increases, analysts said. Traditionally, whenever
the central bank stops taking money out of circulation by raising borrowing costs, cash begins to flow into
various investments, especially stocks.
Major U.S. indexes are showing some strong signals: Stocks hit 4 1/2-year highs last month, with the Dow
Jones Industrial Average nearing the 11000 mark.
But foreign shares are also doing well, including many emerging markets. India's Bombay Sensex 30 has
leapt 13.6%, or 1069.29 points, to 8961.61 since Nov. 1. China's Hang Seng Index has risen 5.7%, or 814.01
points, to 15200.38. Among other gainers, Russian shares have climbed 11%, Brazilian shares 8% and South
Korean shares 5%, as measured by those markets' respective benchmark indexes.
"Investors are getting increasingly global, so even when U.S. investors get bullish, it doesn't just benefit our
domestic markets," said David P. Prokupek, chief executive of the asset-management firm Geronimo
Partners.
Elsewhere over the past month, Japan's Nikkei 225 Stock Average has soared 13.3%, or 1815.10 points, to
15421.6, and France's CAC-40 is up 4.5%, or 200.01 points, to 4636.46 since Nov. 1.
Analysts said a continued slide in crude-oil prices, from highs around $70 a barrel over the summer to less
than $60 now, has bolstered various stock markets around the world. High energy prices are usually
considered a threat to consumers, who may cut back on their purchases of other items to pay for fuel if
energy prices stay too high for too long.
James Bianco, president of Bianco Research, said another factor that bodes well for stocks is relatively low
long-term interest rates in the U.S.
Paradoxically, he said gold's recent rally, which most analysts have attributed to strong physical demand for
metal as well as pockets of inflation jitters, could also continue. Futures have gained 8%, or $37.60, to
$503.30 a troy ounce since Nov. 1. Usually, the Fed's raising of interest rates hurts gold prices, because it
increases the value of the dollar and reduces investors' appetite to use the yellow metal as an alternative
store of value.
However, Mr. Bianco said the latest rally could be sustained by purchases by hedge funds and other
deep-pocket institutions that usually invest in more actively traded assets, like stocks, and want to use small
weightings of gold as a safeguard in their portfolios.
"You've got a tidal wave of money going into relatively illiquid investments," said Mr. Bianco.
Even among the investment sectors that lost ground over the last month, the declines have not been as
dramatic as the winners' gains.
One notable decliner has been the Baltic Dry Index, a measure of shipping prices. It is off nearly 11%, or 341
points, to 2772 since Nov. 1. A downturn in the Baltic Dry Index often presages a decline in industrial and
agricultural commodities. Those commodities have been rallying for more than three years, so a pullback
could be a welcome development that would ease inflation worries. Indexes tracking the prices of corporate
and government bonds globally have also fared poorly recently, with several indicators down about 2%.

Go Figure: Zeroing In on Market Milestones --- The Round-Numbers Approach To Making Decisions Can Have A Real Impact on Trading
Abreast of the Market
Go Figure: Zeroing In on Market Milestones --- The Round-Numbers Approach To Making Decisions
Can Have A Real Impact on Trading
By Aaron Lucchetti
1,222 words
5 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WALL STREET TRADERS and bankers love zeros this time of year -- at the end of their bonus checks.
When it comes to big, round numbers, investors are more ambivalent.
Take 11000. The Dow Jones Industrial Average has been peering at that lofty milestone for more than four
years and has been within striking distance of it for days, but investors can't seem to push it over the top. The
same had been true of gold until it broke the $500-an-ounce mark last week for the first time since 1987 and
Japan's Nikkei 225 Stock Average until it settled above 15000 points Thursday for the first time in nearly five
years.
Many analysts dismiss the focus on such milestones as numerology that doesn't carry much weight in stock
picking or gauging the economy's health. But the approach of round numbers has real impact on trading
because many investors base real decisions on them. If the industrial average hits 11000, some figure, many
will sell to solidify profits, so maybe it is better to sell now -- a mind-set that knocks down prices. Others figure
momentum is building and rush to buy, boosting prices.
"It's 100% psychological," says Michael Cook, head of Cook Mayer Taylor, an asset-management firm in
Memphis, Tenn. "These are emotional figures, but they're real because" individual investors pay attention to
them, and "the market is made up of a host of individuals."
Last Tuesday, the industrial average climbed within 41 points of 11000, only to retreat amid fears that
inflation could keep the Federal Reserve raising short-term interest rates, potentially crimping the economy
and thereby hurting corporate profits. Thursday, the Dow industrials made another failed run at the milestone
and then weakened Friday, to fall 0.5% to 10877.51.
Of course, investors would welcome the gains a move to 11000 represents. But some analysts point out that
getting above that level could unleash a new wave of selling from investors who have been waiting to get out
for years at a higher price, pushing it right back down again.
"People want to sell at big numbers," says Ralph Acampora, head of Knight Technical Research in Jersey
City, N.J. He says the industrial average will probably push above the milestone by the end of the year, but
not before getting close and falling back a few more times.
Indeed, Robert Basel, a senior trader at Citigroup's New York brokerage arm, says investors will start betting
on stock declines by shorting stocks or stock-index products if the industrial average gets much closer to
11000, providing another impediment to making it over the top. "They'll use it as a guideline," he says. "Every
thousand points is a big deal."
Consider what happened the last time the industrial average flirted with 11000. The blue-chip index last
settled above 11000 on June 7, 2001. But in the prior two years, it had climbed through the mark 19 times,
only to fall back down again. Is the 20th time a charm?
Likewise, the industrial average has cleared the 10000 hurdle 24 times since first doing so in 1999. And after
it settled above 1000 for the first time in 1972, it took an additional 30 tries over the next decade until in 1982
it stayed above the millennial mark for good, according to The Wall Street Journal's Market Data Group.
To Richard McCabe, chief market analyst at Merrill Lynch, the industrial average at 11000 doesn't mean
much more than if the index were at 10999 or at 11001. But he acknowledges that stocks can run out of gas
around numbers like 11000, much like a runner who just crossed the finish line.

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Sometimes these round numbers can prove to be anticlimactic," Mr. McCabe says. "I don't think the market
is ready for a great new advance like it was in 1982." Instead, he sees the industrial average pushing through
11000 in the next few weeks, then falling back below 10000 again next year.
Milestones can stall the market as buyers and sellers use the new digits as an excuse to reconsider their
investments. George Gero, vice president of global futures at RBC Capital Markets, says gold hitting $500
could prompt more mining companies to sell future production of the metal at today's prices, essentially
putting a ceiling on the price.
On the other side, new buyers can be drawn to the momentum of a stock index or commodity hitting a new
peak. It's going to be a "tug of war," says Mr. Gero.
Stock-market bulls say that even though the industrial average at 11000 has been a struggle, stocks are
cheaper today than when the index last hit the mark. And not all milestones are a struggle. The industrial
average plowed through 5000, 6000, 7000, 8000 and 9000 with little trouble.
The index hitting a new milestone would give investors "a psychological boost," says Kurt Wolfgruber, chief
investment officer of OppenheimerFunds. In general, milestones give investors hope that more gains could
follow and inspire some fear that they will miss out if they sit out. If "we got to this one, we can now go to the
next one," Mr. Wolfgruber says.
With the Nikkei 225 Stock Average hitting 15000 Thursday and surging nearly 2% higher Friday, Mr.
Wolfgruber says that move is the most important of the recent milestones. "You're almost hearing a
consensus that Japan has finally turned the corner," he says.
Indeed, of the three major milestones, the Nikkei has the most ground to catch up. Even if the Dow industrials
hit 11000, it would stand about 6% below its record 11722.98 set in January 2000. Gold's bold move puts it
about 40% below its 1980 high of $850 an ounce. But the Nikkei still stands about 60% under its record peak
38915.87 in 1989. In such markets, milestones may unleash latent selling pressure from those who "bought
at a previous level and now want to break even," says Knight's Mr. Acampora.
Mr. Acampora and Merrill's Mr. McCabe, who analyze historical movements to help interpret where prices will
go next, say gold's move is most significant because it puts the price at a decades-long high compared with a
near 4 1/2-year peak for the Dow industrials and a five-year crest for the Nikkei.
It is somewhat unusual for gold and the industrial average to be approaching new highs at the same time.
Gold prices usually rise with inflation, something stock investors don't like. When stocks turned in
tremendous gains in the 1980s and 1990s, gold skidded. But this time, gold is rising in part because of
demand from India and China, says Mr. Acampora.
But the analyst warns that further gold-price gains will fan inflation concerns. And Mr. McCabe says gold
has the best chance of the three milestone hitters to keep rising in coming months.
Inflation worrywarts -- and milestone mavens -- are also watching copper, a key industrial commodity, which
recently topped $2 a pound, and platinum, which recently hit $1,000 an ounce.

A Sustained Gold Rush May Pan Out
Deals &Deal Makers
A Sustained Gold Rush May Pan Out
By Alison Guerriere Ciaccio Dow Jones Newswires
674 words
5 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors propelled gold prices above $500 an ounce last week, but many industry experts wonder if the
18-year highs are sustainable.
After surpassing $500 an ounce in December 1987, gold lost its luster and fell sharply in early 1988, reaching
a low of $424 an ounce that February. By the end of 1988, gold regularly was dipping below $400 an ounce.
During the next 10 years, gold never traded higher than $430 an ounce, and by 1999, it sank to $252.50 -- its
lowest level since 1979.
The bull market for gold has been running for the past five years, as gold prices have climbed more than
77%.
Many industry experts said the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks propelled the rally as gold's status as a haven
investment gained appeal amid increased terrorism fears and geopolitical turmoil. After that, the gold market
was trading on the euro-dollar relationship, but inflation fears and consumer demand are now the main
drivers.
While gold prices typically have traded inversely to the dollar, both have moved higher this year.
Market experts contend that gold has partially broken away from the dollar and is rising on its own demand
fundamentals. That, in turn, will keep the price of gold sustainable at the $500 level, said Michael Cuggino,
president and chief executive of Permanent Portfolio Family of Funds.
"We are seeing a slow devaluation of the unit value of the dollar and are seeing assets like gold and silver
appreciating," said Mr. Cuggino, whose portfolio structure is 20% gold-based.
For the short term, the gold market will remain volatile, Mr. Cuggino added, with downside corrections likely.
But he said distinct differences from 1987 -- like that year's stock-market crash and a threatening
recessionary period mixed with high interest rates at that time -- are central to expectations that this bull
market for gold is more sustainable than the one in the late 1980s.
However, Jeffrey Christian, managing director of CPM Group, a precious-metals research and consulting
company based in New York, said the situation isn't that different from 18 years ago.
In 1987, "the dollar was bottoming out and people were rushing into gold and out of the dollar. Even banks
were buying significant amounts of gold. We are seeing a very similar situation now," Mr. Christian said.
According to a recent study by the World Gold Council, an industry association of the world's leading
gold-mining companies, investment demand for gold rose 56% in the third quarter and is expected to
increase further.
In 1987, fears of an economic slowdown were evident but never came to fruition, and gold prices sank. The
rally in the late 1980s was short, but despite his calls of similarities, Mr. Christian said this bull run for gold
has lasted longer than the quick run-up to $500 an ounce during 1987.
"We are also seeing increased investment demand," Mr. Christian noted, adding that the steady run-up for
gold over the past five years "doesn't mean it's a cycle that never ends."
For the near term, Mr. Christian expects gold to back off and head toward $460 an ounce, which he calls a
"bullish level." Market participants who got into gold when prices were much lower may be more likely to take
profits at these high levels, he added.

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The rising gold price also may dent thriving consumer demand, Mr. Christian said, as jewelers may be less
likely to buy at current levels.
Analysts at UBS Investment Research have increased their price forecasts, citing inflation concerns, a
contraction in mine supplies and an acceleration in investment demand. UBS raised its average gold-price
forecast for 2005 to $441 an ounce from $434. For 2006, UBS forecast an average gold price of $520 an
ounce, up 14.2% from a previous forecast of $455, and for 2007, an average price of $500, an increase of
15% from $435.

The Week in Review
The Week in Review
559 words
3 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BlackBerrys Keep Buzzing
A court ruling had millions of BlackBerry users, and companies that depend on the email devices, worried
about a shutdown. Research In Motion's use of certain technology infringes on patents owned by NTP Inc.;
the companies have been unable to reach a settlement. This ruling opens the way for NTP to seek
reinstatement of a court injunction barring U.S. BlackBerry sales and service. RIM is still battling -- it also won
a court ruling -- and claims it can bypass NTP's patents, anyway. Still, with 625,000 new subscribers in the
latest quarter, and Oprah Winfrey plugging the device on TV, it seems doubtful RIM would risk a blackout.
News From the Front
President Bush acknowledged missteps in Iraq but said progress continued and, in response to withdrawal
calls, said "we will never accept anything less than complete victory." He emphasized improvements by Iraqi
forces without giving a timetable for U.S. troop reductions, and said the administration has a strategy to win
the war without offering specifics. The speech was aimed at crumbling public support, but U.S. allies could
have used it, too. Bulgaria and Ukraine begin withdrawing their combined 1,250 troops this month, and a
half-dozen others are debating reductions.
Stuffing Those Stockings
U.S. consumers shopped fiercely over Thanksgiving weekend but results overall were mixed. A shopping
center group reported sales at chain stores open at least a year rose 3.5% while results at Nordstrom and
Saks disappointed. Deep discounts -- such as $398 laptops at Wal-Mart Stores, $48 leather jackets at J.C.
Penney -- attracted big crowds. Whether retailers can maintain margins at those prices is one question;
whether shoppers can afford the pace is another. The good news: A late Hanukkah, starting Christmas night,
could provide a boost.
Having It Your Way
The FCC and the cable industry faced off on "a la carte" programming, which would allow viewers to choose
individual channels rather than pre-packaged bundles. In a Senate hearing, the FCC said buying channels
separately or in themed tiers would save consumers money and offer family-friendly options. The industry
argued their model subsidizes smaller networks that would get squeezed out by a-la-carte pricing. The FCC
can't force its will on cable operators, but -- with Cablevision Systems breaking ranks and announcing its
support for a la carte -- it may not need to.
Inflation Stalks the Continent
The European Central Bank raised interest rates for the first time in five years, citing inflation fears for the
jump to 2.25% from 2%. Business, labor and national governments opposed the move, fearing it might stall
an economic recovery. With the ECB lacking the stature of the U.S. Federal Reserve, and President
Jean-Claude Trichet that of Chairman Alan Greenspan, this move could help establish its credibility in
steering the economy. If growth falters, expect meddling from the capitals to increase.

Dow Industrials Fall Short of 11000 Mark
Friday's Markets
Dow Industrials Fall Short of 11000 Mark
By Peter A. McKay
471 words
3 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS WERE mixed both for Friday and for the week, in a bit of an anticlimax to the Dow Jones Industrial
Average's recent flirtation with the 11000 benchmark.
Analysts said the blue-chip indicator was due for a pullback following Thursday's heavy gains, but could
resume its bullish run before the end of the year and close above 11000 for the first time since 2001. Recent
reports on economic growth and manufacturing have been encouraging, they said, though fresh jobs data
Friday drew mixed reviews.
The Dow industrials fell 35.06 points, or 0.3%, to finish at 10877.51, off 0.5% for the week. The Nasdaq
Composite Index gained 6.2, or 0.3%, to end at 2273.37, up 0.5% for the week. And the broad Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index edged up 0.41, or 0.03%, to 1265.08, off 0.3% for the week.
Afternoon buying reversed what had been intraday losses Friday in the Nasdaq and S&P, but it couldn't push
blue-chip stocks into the black.
All three indicators are up for the year so far, about 4% each for the Nasdaq and the S&P 500, and 0.9% for
the Dow.
The U.S. Labor Department announced that the economy added 215,000 nonfarm jobs in November, in line
with economists' expectations of 210,000.
Strategist Jim Russell of Fifth Third Asset Management said the jobs report wasn't the most impressive of
recent encouraging economic signals, but the overall picture remains positive.
"We view the market as probably vulnerable to a little bit of a pullback for now, but that doesn't derail the
broader story," he said.
In light of the Fed's steady stream of interest-rate rises, Mr. Russell said he has been "astonished" by gold's
recent strength. The metal usually is considered a haven when people lose confidence in other investments.
By effectively taking dollars out of circulation through raising rates, the Fed's moves typically would bolster
the dollar and hurt gold. Gold futures rose 0.2%, or 80 cents, to $503.30 a troy ounce on the Comex division
of the New York Mercantile Exchange, up 2.2% on the week.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.53 billion shares changed hands, 1,749
issues advanced and 1,557 declined.
Bond were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note fell 1/32 to yield 4.521%. The 30-year bond fell 1/32 to yield
4.72%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 120.54 yen, up from 120.44 yen Thursday, while the
euro fell to $1.1717 from $1.1733.

Investing in Metals Hits a Fever Pitch --- Wall Street Woos The Small Investor, But Know the Risk
Personal Finance
Investing in Metals Hits a Fever Pitch --- Wall Street Woos The Small Investor, But Know the Risk
By Colleen DeBaise Dow Jones Newswires
822 words
3 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT'S LIKE THE 1980s all over again.
Gold closed Friday at $503.30 an ounce, its highest price since Feb. 21, 1983. It is up 15% since Jan. 1.
Platinum, meanwhile, topped $1,000 an ounce earlier this week, its highest level in more than two decades.
Analysts credit a number of factors for precious metals' sharp rise. Demand from investors, afraid of rising
inflation and looking to protect their portfolios by diversifying, is helping to drive up the price. Increased
industrial use of the metals is also contributing, as is demand in developing nations such as China and India
for their use as jewelry and as a store of wealth.
As precious-metals fever heats up, Wall Street is rolling out more products geared to small investors,
including exchange-traded funds and so-called structured notes. And some financial advisers are steering
their clients into precious or industrial metals as a way to diversify their portfolios and protect them from
inflation.
Investors, for their part, have been pouring money into mutual funds that specialize in precious metals.
Through the end of October, $826 million flowed into three precious-metals funds offered by Fidelity
Investments, Vanguard Group and Franklin Templeton, as compared to $101 million for all of 2004, according
to TrimTabs Investment Research.
The investment-research firm Morningstar Inc. lists American Century Global Gold and Vanguard Precious
Metals and Mining as its favorite precious-metals funds. Both funds invest primarily in mining companies,
which tend to track the value of the underlying metals but are also subject to the risks any company faces.
Buying an exchange-traded fund that directly holds metal assets -- such as streetTracks Gold Shares -- is the
simplest way for individuals to get exposure. Exchange-traded funds resemble index-tracking mutual funds,
but trade on an exchange like a stock.
Yet precious-metal investments are notoriously volatile, and can have heavy tax consequences when you try
to sell them. If you're investing in actual metals -- or in notes tied to them -- they can also be fairly illiquid, or
difficult to sell. And if gold and platinum prices are nearing a peak, as some experts fear, the dangers to
small investors are even more pronounced.
"We have investors who think they want [metals] but don't understand the risk of volatility in the marketplace,"
says Fred Whaley, managing director of Raymond James Financial Inc.'s alternative-investments group in St.
Petersburg, Fla.
Historically, gold has provided stability during times of confusion, but many don't like how its price can
languish or soar. Inflation fears in the late 1970s drove the price of gold to more than $800 an ounce, but
interest dwindled as inflation fell -- and many investors suffered big losses.
While some are optimistic that gold will continue to rise, other financial planners say it's best to stay away.
"I've never been a gold bug," says Neil McCarthy, a financial planner in Roswell, Ga., who prefers oil and real
estate to gold. "It's more speculative than other commodities."
Some investors prefer silver, arguing that it's undervalued relative to gold. Barclays Global Investors, a unit of
Barclays PLC, is planning to launch a new exchange-traded fund tracking silver called iShares Silver Trust, a
cousin to its gold ETF, iShares Comex Gold Trust. The firm filed a registration statement with the Securities
and Exchange Commission in June for the new product, but wouldn't comment on a timeline for its launch.

--- Page 18 ---
Page 18 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gains in ETFs, though, are taxed at a higher rate, as gold and silver are considered "collectibles" and
investors are treated as if they owned the underlying metals. Advisers suggest buying ETFs in tax-deferred
accounts, such as an individual retirement account.
Individuals can buy gold-mining stocks directly, but advisers say investing in a single stock doesn't provide
the same protection from market tumult as a vehicle that invests in a broad mix of metals producers or
directly owns various metals.
For investors tempted by precious metals, advisers suggest keeping these rules in mind:
Don't invest in coins. An investor can buy gold or silver coins through a precious-metals dealer, but that
requires expertise and proper security. Simply put, "coins, bullion -- it's just too hard to store," says Matthew
Chope, a financial planner in Southfield, Mich.
Diversify. With worries that the easy money has already been made in metals like gold, diversification is key.
Rather than pick one metal, consider others, such as platinum and silver.
Don't get in too deep. No more than 5% to 10% of your portfolio should be in alternative investments.
Precious metals in particular can be risky and volatile. Investors should be prepared to weather the ups and
downs.

Eleanor Laise contributed to this article.

Holiday Book Guide: The Holidays Are an Open Book
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Holiday Book Guide: The Holidays Are an Open Book
4,744 words
2 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AT ANY TIME of the year it is possible to find books that -- in their visual audacity, exotic display and
immodest price -- defy the normal rules of book buying. Prudence requires a covetous riffling-through and no
more. But surely one point of the holidays is to throw off such cautious habit, to reach beyond the prissy and
manageable, to indulge in the magnificence of otherwise forbidden books -- if only for the sake of finding a gift
for a friend. In exactly this generous spirit, our in-house critics have perused the gift books in various
categories and chosen a few of the notable ones. More than a few, actually.
Photography
By Tunku Varadarajan
ONCE UPON A TIME, portrait photography was regarded as a source of cultural doom. Baudelaire, for
instance, reacting to early daguerreotypes, gnashed his dentures thus: "Our squalid society rushed,
Narcissus to a man, to gaze at its trivial image on a scrap of metal."
We remain Narcissuses to a man, but -- marvelous to relate -- our images are not always trivial.
Latter-day Baudelaires need only look at "Disfarmer: The Vintage Prints" (Powerhouse, 240 pages, $60) to be
cured of their sourness. I confess that I did not know the photographer, Mike Disfarmer (1884-1959) and so
approached his work with no baggage of preconception. I was bewitched. Disfarmer was a studio
photographer in Heber Springs, Ark., from 1917 to 1956, and his work brings to life not merely a range of
modest people who posed for him but also a defunct, sepia era, when small towns such as Heber Springs
were their own private specks-on-the-map.
Unlike the exhaustively celebrated portraits brought together in "Richard Avedon: In the American West"
(Abrams, 184 pages, $75) -- which were commissioned for a museum exhibition and so bear the stamp of a
premeditated artistic mission -- Disfarmer's pictures were intended as commercial transactions, for sale to the
subjects who posed for him. Yet they cede nothing to Avedon's in their excellence of eye and composition
and are undoubtedly more authentic as American documentary.
On his photographic road trip through the American West in the 1980s, Avedon strove mightily -- and
successfully -- for effect, seeking for ways to disconcert his audience. Hence his pictures of drifters and
soot-smeared coalminers, his tattooed riffraff and his flashes of ghoulish humor. Who can forget Ronald
Fischer, the beekeeper, who has a hive-full of insects crawling all over his bare body?
Disfarmer, by contrast, had more than just a chance encounter with his subjects, or a choreographed one. He
saw them, presumably, in everyday settings -- at the drug store, the bar, the diner. This quotidian contact
ensured that he brought simplicity, humility and diplomacy to his undoubted technical ability with a camera.
The result is a series of graceful portraits, always respectful of the Little People depicted -- much in the
manner, say, of Seydou Keita, the Malian photographer whose lifetime's work in small-town Africa has been
rightly feted in recent years.
None of the pictures in the Disfarmer book has a caption; each was painstakingly retrieved from private
albums and households in the area where he worked. One is left -- somewhat pleasurably -- to imagine who
these people were. Is there a Sapphic quality to the three girls (one rather dishy) who pose, bodies pressed
together, on page 65? How on earth did that pleasant-looking fellow on page 94 end up with such a frumpy
wife? Was the languid couple on page 179 -- he well-fed, she well-groomed -- the happiest in Heber Springs?
Or did they cheat on each other and smile merely for Disfarmer's lens?
Although the best photography is often intimate in proportion, the medium is supple enough to astonish us on
scales that are vaster than a man's brow or the breadth of a matron's bosom. Panoramas have always been
a particular source of wonderment for viewers, and the earliest feats of such portrayal -- the length of a

--- Page 20 ---
Page 20 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
battlefield, the span of an ancient ruin -- were often minor technical miracles performed on instruments that
resisted the wishes of a photographer more readily than they yielded to his desires.
Today's cameras and lenses have made photography much less a feat of engineering than it once was. Such
relative ease has only spurred the more audacious among our global throng of cameramen to newer and
greater heights -- sometimes literally. Robert B. Haas hired a plane and flew over the Dark Continent with his
camera, and the fruit of his sky-borne labors -- "Through the Eyes of the Gods: An Aerial Vision of Africa"
(National Geographic, 208 pages, $50) -- is a book of photographs so unlike any other that I've seen as to
suggest the imminent outbreak of copycat flights by other photographers, soaring over Australia, Europe,
Latin America and all lands where hostile ground armies won't shoot down a seemingly aberrant Cessna.
What is remarkable about Mr. Haas's pictures is not their panoptic grandeur: That, at some level, was
predictable. Instead, and paradoxically, it is the detail that these pictures capture. Why is there a single, lone
wildebeest in the midst of a stampeding herd of zebra? Who is that white woman in a cotton dress at the
edge of an immense fish market in rural Senegal?
Fewer questions born of such simple curiosity are provoked by "The Past From Above: Aerial Photographs of
Archaeological Sights" (J. Paul Getty Museum, 415 pages, $65), a sumptuous collection of shots of Roman
amphitheaters, Greek temples, ancient ports and pyramids, and Asiatic temples and stupas taken over the
course of 40 years by Georg Gerster. Not only is the book's purpose more conventionally documentary but
each of its photographs has a full and informative commentary accompanying it.
This helpful content is refreshing, for text is often the bane of photo books. "The Ganges: Along Sacred
Waters" (Thames &Hudson, 336 pages, $50) is an example, riddled as it is with commentary that veers
between banality and psychotropic excess. Mercifully, the pictures taken by the photographer, Aldo Pavan,
are miles better than his prose. Some, in fact, are extraordinarily sensitive, such as that of four boys playing
cricket in rural Bihar, in eastern India, or that of a group of Bengali schoolgirls, whose muted coquetry is
caught in a flurry of saris and red ribbons.
India is one of those countries -- Ireland is another -- where it is hard to take a picture that does not engage
the eye, so one tends to judge photographers who make these countries their subjects a little more exactingly
than others. That said, "Magnum Ireland" (Thames &Hudson, 256 pages, $60) hits the very highest note. This
collection -- a compilation of photographs from the 1950s to the present day culled from the Magnum photo
agency's archives -- is a book so good that I could find not one picture over which I did not wish to linger.
Inevitably -- heartbreakingly -- the photos from "The Troubles" of the 1970s and 1980s are the most arresting,
with the Irish here at their shoddiest. But in the midst of the sectarian squalor there are glimpses of cheer and
decency. In one photograph, a British soldier (black... from the West Indies perhaps?) crouches behind a
flower-bush in a small suburban plot, his rifle at the ready. Behind him, her quizzical face on the brink of a
smile, is an Irish housewife mowing the lawn. Beckett couldn't have made this up. Nor Joyce, for that matter.
Art
By Eric Gibson
AN ART BOOK so large you need a handle to lift it? Yes, it's come to that. This year's coffee-table sensation
is Frank Zollner's "Sandro Botticelli" (Prestel, 320 pages, $165). The book comes in a box to which the
publisher has kindly attached a handle. And no wonder. At nearly 13 pounds, this is one stocking stuffer that
will throw Santa's back out.
Botticelli, the 15th-century Florentine painter, is best known for his "Birth of Venus" -- and has paid a price for
it. That painting, located in Florence's Uffizi Gallery, is so famous as to have virtually obscured everything
else by him. It also highlights one of his few real weaknesses: a tendency toward the saccharine. But
Botticelli was also a masterly painter of religious subjects, an innovative portraitist, an accomplished
landscape painter and, above all, an artist capable of conveying intense emotions. Mr. Zollner, a German art
historian, has provided a substantial text along with a complete catalog of Botticelli's paintings in a book that
wants for nothing in the way of illustrations.
Rockefeller Center is widely (and justifiably) recognized as an architectural masterpiece, but it is also the
largest repository of public art in the country, if not the world, a fact less well known. Dozens of artists have
been commissioned over the years to create paintings, sculptures, reliefs and other adornments throughout
the 11-acre complex. Some of the most famous names were signed up at the time of the center's
construction, including Isamu Noguchi, Gaston Lachaise, Paul Manship and, not least, Diego Rivera, whose
mural was famously canceled because the artist insisted on including an image of Lenin.
"The Art of Rockefeller Center" by Christine Roussel (Norton, 320 pages, $59.95) is surely the most
comprehensive work on the decoration of these buildings that we have yet had. Ms. Roussel goes building by
building, artist by artist, giving the history of each and a critical analysis. It turns out that Rivera's was not the

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
only work in which politics intervened. Giacomo Manzu's 1965 relief over the entrance to the Palazzo d'Italia
was made to replace another from 1935. The earlier one featured the fascist emblem of rods bound together
with an ax.
When we think of sea paintings, the names of past masters like Winslow Homer and Fitz Hugh Lane come to
mind. "Wooden Ships &Iron Men" by Reese Palley and Marilyn Arnold Palley (Quantuck Lane, 175 pages,
$59.95) traces the remarkable career of one of their successors. In 1972 Thomas Hoyne, a commercial artist,
was diagnosed with cancer and given two years to live. Determined to make the most of the time left to him,
he decided to paint what really interested him -- sailing ships at sea, and he ended up painting some of the
most compelling works ever done in the genre.
His paintings combine the illustrator's eye for descriptive detail with the fine artist's striving to communicate
something larger -- in this case, the demon power of water. Mr. Hoyne had served in the Navy during World
War II, and it's clear from his paintings that he spent a great deal of time during his service observing water
and, so to speak, how it behaved.
For many painters water is more or less just what a boat sits in. To them it's all about the surface -- the
waves, the spray, the play of light. Hoyne saw all this and more. Water is an elemental force in his work. Its
fearsome power originates below the surface and then pushes itself into the visible world. While we may
marvel at Hoyne's ability to capture watercraft down to the smallest detail, we never to lose sight of the fact
that his vessels, however substantial -- and their crews, however competent -- exist at nature's pleasure,
forever hovering at the edge of destruction. In the end, Hoyne lived far longer than his allotted two years. To
find out why you'll have to read this extraordinary book.
Is it possible that there is no book in print on Frederic Edwin Church (1826-1900), one of America's greatest
painters? The publicists at Yale University Press claim so, and a quick check of Amazon proves them correct.
So it is a good thing that we now have "Frederic Church" by John K. Howat (Yale, 224 pages, $50), a superb
and beautifully illustrated study of the life and work of this landscape painter.
Church is known as a member of the Hudson River School, but the label hardly does him justice. Some of his
masterpieces depict such varied subjects as South American vistas and icebergs off the coast of
Newfoundland. They are vast, sprawling paintings that depict flora and fauna with minute precision while
communicating an almost overwhelming sense of nature's grandeur. Church also looks ahead to later
generations of American landscape painters, such as Winslow Homer and Marsden Hartley, by taking the
Maine shore as a subject. Mr. Howat gives us Church in full -- as an artist, man and entrepreneur. One of the
most interesting sections of the book show how Church's shrewd marketing of "Heart of the Andes" put both
the painting and its artist on the map.
Histories of art normally take the form of chronological surveys of periods and styles. But materials play an
important role in the history of art, too, although they don't always receive the attention they deserve. Which
makes Meher McArthur's "The Arts of Asia: Materials, Techniques, Styles" (Thames &Hudson, 256 pages,
$50) most welcome, all the more so since materials are perhaps a more important aesthetic element in Asian
art than in Western. The book takes 10 substances -- jade, silk, porcelain, lacquer, ivory, bamboo, paper,
gold, wood and stone -- and devotes a chapter to each, exploring physical properties, aesthetic appeal and
the works of art the each was used to create.
This approach gives us a keen appreciation of what artists have to go through to bring us their treasures.
Jade, for example, is exceptionally difficult to carve, though you'd never know it from the abundance of
beautiful jade objects in the Asian canon. In the chapter on lacquer, we learn that carved lacquer -- e.g.,
those elegant red vases whose every square inch is covered in relief -- is exceptionally difficult and
time-consuming to create, requiring the application of as many as 300 layers. The coating itself is derived
from the sap of a tree and is used both to make an object more beautiful and to protect it from insects and
other damaging forces. "Assuming each layer requires up to four or five days to dry," Mr. McArthur writes, "a
single piece can take as long as two years to prepare before the carver can begin."
We can't all be Frederic Church, of course, but "The Art of Botanical Painting," by Margaret Stevens (Collins,
144 pages, $29.95) can help us pretend that we are. Botanical illustration is a wonderful hybrid of science
and art, and this book is designed for the person who has admired a plant in someone's garden -- or a
botanical illustration itself -- and wanted to try his hand at capturing nature's beauty. Great effort has gone
into making the subject as unintimidating as possible The book offers a brief history of the genre, a primer on
plant anatomy and drawing techniques, and chapter headings that amount to a step-by-step lesson. As you'd
expect, the book is lavishly illustrated. If in the end you don't feel up to trying your hand as an artist, you'll
derive pleasure from studying the pictures.
Travel
By Michael Philips

--- Page 22 ---
Page 22 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
HISTORY RECORDS that in 2003 the photographer Michael Hawley -- with the help of MIT and the Kingdom
of Bhutan -- published the world's largest book. Weighing almost 135 pounds, it measured five feet by seven
feet. Not exactly the perfect object for a coffee table. "Bhutan: A Visual Odyssey Across the Last Himalayan
Kingdom" (Friendly Planet, 216 pages, $100) presents a reduced replica of that immense photo-book, as well
as the story behind it.
It's hard to know what to make of a country whose main economic indicator is said to be its Gross National
Happiness (high, as these things go) and whose main economic asset may well be its pristine landscape,
ranging from mountains to tropical jungles. Bhutan has somehow managed to avoid the political upheaval
experienced by other Himalayan nations, such as Nepal and Tibet. It is the Earth's last Shangri-La.
Over the course of about a year, Mr. Hawley and 10 other principal photographers embarked on four
expeditions to Bhutan, viewing it from helicopter, horse, yak, car and foot. They ended up, ably and
wonderfully, revealing the exotic appeal of this unique dot on the globe. The country hasn't modernized much,
and perhaps it never will. Except for a rarely spotted car or a lighted hospital, the photographs give few signs
of anything that could not have existed in the 18th century or earlier. Bhutan, one can't help feeling, is the
ideal destination for anyone needing a break from his BlackBerry or cellphone.
China, by contrast, is home to more and more such devices. It is also, unlike neighboring Bhutan, a
much-visited place, filled with landmarks -- from the Great Wall to Emperor Qin Shihuang's mausoleum (with
its terracotta army) to the Forbidden City in Beijing. But of course there is much, much more to the country
than such tourist attractions. "China: Its Most Scenic Places" (Reader's Digest, 247 pages, $30) ventures off
the beaten track, offering a photographic look at 50 of the country's most charming towns from four vastly
different regions.
On display are remote villages, quaint views of the nearby countryside and, not least, majestic vistas. The
Little White Mountain Range in the northern-most province of Heilongjiang affords conditions of evident cold
and desolation. But the Manchurian people have called it home for millennia. To see Tianchi (Sky Lake) in the
summer, with the alpine-like flowers in bloom, is to imagine the compensating delicacy of such a place. The
Great Gorge in the Changbai Shan province -- in northern China -- offers a very different scene: The Jinjiang
River has carved out a rocky ravine 490 feet deep. In western China is Xinjiang, an almost ethereal "city."
The photographs seem to show houses, temples, streets and marketplaces -- but none is the work of man.
Wind and rain have eroded the plateau to look like a village. Locals call it Ghost City, because ghostly cries
run through it.
Quite the opposite sort of habitat -- a very, very crowded one -- can be found in Hiroji Kubota's "Japan"
(Norton, 218 pages, $59.95). To judge by an aerial shot of Tokyo, not a square inch of the city is empty:
Superhighways wind through an endless terrain of fertile concrete, from which rows of steel office buildings
seem to grow. Yet on the previous page we see a gorgeous wooded retreat in Kyoto. Every shade of green
imaginable is on display -- and one or two otherwise unimaginable. The eye is finally drawn to an orange fish
swimming under a moss-covered stone bridge.
As the text of the book states, and as cliche would have it, modern Japan is a land of contradictions. We see
an image, from a Tokyo auto show, of the world's smallest electronic car, built to "wrap around" its passenger,
and elsewhere the photo of a masked technician holding a single-lens reflect camera, one of the country's
many technological accomplishments. But not far away are images of traditional artisans handling kimono
fabric, painted pottery and the implements of a centuries-old tea ceremony. The country as a whole seems to
look to the future wide-eyed and excited, cultivating an almost defensive cuteness in its pop culture. But
formality is everywhere evident, and history too: The book's final two pages show a beautiful fireworks display
over Hiroshima.
The text of "Japan" is spare and discreet, tucked minimally into its back pages. Florian Schulz's "Yellowstone
to Yukon" (Mountaineers Books, 196 pages, $34.95) displays mostly uncaptioned photographs, too, and
beautiful ones. But discreet is not the word for the text on offer. With essays from biologists and
conservationists -- and a groan-inducing epilogue by Robert F. Kennedy Jr. -- "Yellowstone to Yukon" intends
to spread the Good Word of Environmentalism, heavy-handedly.
But the photographs more than make up for the pain. From the northwest corner of Wyoming to the
Alaska-Canada border, Mr. Schulz captures buffalo roaming, bears foraging and, at one point, a badger
feeding on an unfortunate squirrel; there are as well magnificent glaciers and waterfalls and mountain lakes
and golden shores at the end of rainbows. Our national parks, it is clear, do serve a purpose. Most of the
book's images come from them.
There is nothing like a good long walk for seeing nature up-close, in a national park or elsewhere. "Classic
Hikes of the World" (Norton, 223 pages, $39.95) details 23 possible routes. Peter Potterfield, a journalist and
publisher, draws on his own travels to supply superb photographs of far-off places and to gauge the
challenges of getting there on foot. At the beginning of each chapter he says how long each hike takes, the

--- Page 23 ---
Page 23 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
distance it traverses and the difficulty is poses both physically and psychologically. Among the toughest, not
surprisingly, are routes along parts of Mount Kilimanjaro, K2 and Everest. The Fitz Roy Grand Tour in
Argentina, by contrast, sounds manageable by mere mortals. There are no animals determined to do you in,
Mr. Potterfield says, and no dangerous cliffs. The biggest hazard is the "fickle Patagonian weather." No one
should mind a little rain.
Architecture &Design
By Lisa Rossi
`HIP" IS A STATE of mind, a social pose, a gauge of coolness -- and, it turns out, a trademark. "Hip Hotels:
Atlas" is one in a series of books, brought out by Thames &Hudson, featuring far-off places of striking design
and modern comfort -- destinations that "provide stories for many a dinner party to come," as the introduction
modestly claims. This sophisticated guidebook -- with text and photographs by Herbert Ypma (522 pages,
$50) -- encourages travelers to explore destinations off the beaten path. It then presents a variety of remote
but luxurious lodgings in which to stay.
The book ranges across every continent, offering short, scene-setting texts to describe each hotel and its
history and then displaying brilliant thumbnail and full-page photographs of facades, walkways, vistas and
architectural interiors. Tucked demurely at the bottom of a page, for each hotel, is the kind of vital information
that a traveler, as opposed to a mere book-reader, might need. The room rates are often listed, demurely, in
foreign currency.
In Greece, Mr. Ypma suggests that one stay at the Katikies on the island of Santorini, a blinding white
configuration of dome-topped structures stacked vertically on a cliff. North of the Arctic Circle, in Sweden, he
finds an elaborately designed structure whose interior components -- pillars, bed frames, chapel and cinema
-- are carved from snow and ice. Far away, but close in spirit, is Egypt's Adrere Amellal, snuggled into the
sand of the North African Sahara and constructed of salt, palm timber and baked mud. There is no electricity
or phone service. Perhaps there is tranquility instead.
As ascetic as certain hip hotels are, the people who can afford to visit them are unlikely to travel with
rucksacks and duffel bags. At least a few will be carrying Louis Vuitton luggage. Thereby hangs a tale. "Louis
Vuitton: The Birth of Modern Luxury," by Paul-Gerard Pasols (Abrams, 539 pages, $125), tells the
rags-to-riches story of one of the world's great entrepreneurs.
Louis's success began in Paris in 1837 when, freshly arrived from the provinces, he apprenticed to a man
who made (and packed) customized, wooden travel-boxes for the wealthy. After 17 years of honing his skills,
he opened his own store, and the Empress Eugenie, the wife of Napoleon Bonaparte III, became a patron. In
1858, he designed an elegant, slat trunk that, with its flat top, could be stacked in transport, unlike the
dome-topped trunks of the time. A commercial empire was born, and a dynasty. Louis's son designed the
now famous, brown-and-beige Monogram canvas with the interlinked initials "LV." A grandson, in turn,
created a bag for carrying Champagne, a sophisticated sack with a flat bottom and a cinched top. The Noe,
as it was called, eventually became the prototype for a popular handbag made of supple canvas, launching
Louis Vuitton into the fashion industry.
France is known as much for wine as for fashion -- both are part of a centuries-old tradition. Which is one
reason why "Adventurous Wine Architecture" (Images Publishing, 204 pages, $60) passes over French
wineries entirely. Michael Webb is concerned with the new -- with the striking building designs to be found at
modern vineyards in Chile, Australia and elsewhere. His tour of 31 wineries -- accompanied by Erhard
Pfeiffer's crisp, panoramic photographs -- includes a brief history of the vineyards and the wines they produce
and vivid descriptions of their architecture and design details.
We see Santiago Calatrava's graceful Bodegas Ysios, a building nestled into a 160-acre estate in Rioja
Alavesa, Spain. The winery's definitive feature is its rippling roof line, clad in aluminum and playfully
suggesting a snake in the grass. Javier Barba's Stag's Leap Cellars is an organic, cave-like structure of
exposed rock crouched in the hills of Napa Valley, Calif. The subterranean winery is a labyrinth of curving
tunnels converging in a circular piazza. One only hopes that the wine is as interesting as the architecture.
In Japan it is tea, not wine, that matters, and tea is sipped as ceremoniously there as any Chateauneuf du
Pape by a cautious oenophile. "Inspired Design: Japan's Traditional Arts" (5 Continents, 315 pages, $75)
shows photographs of ceramic teabowls from the 16th and 17th centuries -- and a great deal more.
The ideals of Zen Buddhism, Michael Dunn tells us, have influenced every aspect of traditional Japanese life
-- art, architecture, ceramics, garden design. The key is in precise execution and a reverence accorded to
each task, no matter how mundane. Mr. Dunn scrutinizes the essence of Japanese applied art and its
evolution over time, from prehistoric pottery to 10th-century wooden temples to 20th-century brass braziers.
Among the most striking objects is a 19th-century smoking set that includes a pouch made of wood, crocodile
skin, silver and ivory.

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The tatami mat of the Muromachi period (1333-1573) has resonance even today. Made of tightly woven igusa
weeds and rice straw, it measures roughly two meters by one meter. Indeed, the mat's dimensions are used
as the standard unit of measure in contemporary Japanese housing. "For Japanese, a small living space
does not necessarily connote deprivation," says Naomi Pollock in "Modern Japanese House" (Phaidon, 228
pages, $75), an inspiring catalog of innovative, residential Japanese architecture complemented by an array
of photographs.
Ms. Pollock argues that, in contrast to their counterparts in the West, Japanese architects are sensitive to
family social structure and to nature, both of which guide home designs. Restrictive building codes and small
land plots play a part, too. Among the houses Ms. Pollock admires is Masaki Endoh and Masahiro Ikeda's
"Natural Shelter" in Tokyo, a 829-square-foot, arc-shaped structure, situated amid lush foliage, whose
exterior walls are built partly of glass.
Aesthetic harmony was apparently not a concern of actress Jane Mansfield, whose mansion on Sunset
Boulevard featured a heart-and-cupid motif and a bathroom with wall-to-wall, floor-to-ceiling pink shag
carpeting. The "Pink Palace" is just one of the 27 residences included in "Architectural Digest: Hollywood at
Home" (Abrams, 224 pages, $40), a compilation of articles culled from the magazine and edited by Paige
Rense. Cher's "sanctuary" is an Italian-Renaissance-style villa; director George Cukor's, an unpretentious
1930s relic filled with mementos and art. Angelica Houston had her house made to order by sculptor-husband
Robert Graham, to whom she said: "I like arches, domes, white, and plaster." A spectacular three-story,
stucco villa enclosed by a courtyard, just steps from Venice beach, was the answer.
Though most of the stars have California ZIP codes, there are some who don't, such as Greta Garbo, who
filled her New York apartment with personally designed rugs of bold geometric designs and bright colors. If
such a vivid setting defies the dark and reclusive Garbo of stereotype, well, so much the better.

Nikkei, Gold Hit Milestones; Dow Nears One --- Japan's Strengthening Economy And Market Overhauls Propel Stock Index to Close Above 15000
World Stock Markets
Nikkei, Gold Hit Milestones; Dow Nears One --- Japan's Strengthening Economy And Market
Overhauls Propel Stock Index to Close Above 15000
By Yuka Hayashi and Craig Karmin
1,453 words
2 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE GROWING STRENGTH in Japan's economy sent the Nikkei Stock Average to a five-year high, zooming
past the 15000 mark. Sustaining those gains will depend in part on whether Japan can keep moving toward
U.S.-style practices for how companies and markets are run.
The Nikkei hadn't closed above 15000 since December 2000. Yesterday, the 225-stock Nikkei rose 258.35
points, or 1.7%, to 15130.50, leaving it up 32% for the year.
The index finished today's morning session up 193.33 points, or nearly 1.3%, at 15323.83.
The rally is supported by the notion that Japan's economy is finally escaping a stubborn spell of deflation -- a
general fall in prices -- that had haunted the nation for years, and consumers and companies alike are
regaining the confidence needed to keep the economy growing.
Beneath the economic indicators, a raft of deep, structural changes have taken place in Japan's financial
market over the past few years, from improvements in corporate governance to tougher market rules. While
few international fund managers believe financial disclosure and minority-shareholder treatment in Japan are
on par with the U.S., many say the situation is much improved.
"A lot of walls between investors and Japanese companies are being torn down," says Steve Auth, chief
investment officer at Federated Investors in Pittsburgh. "That removes some of the risk from the market."
The changes are making Japanese companies a more attractive investment for a broader audience, including
foreigners and individual investors, and could increase the likelihood that the market will continue to rise over
the long term.
For decades, Japanese companies and banks held each other's shares to cement business ties, a practice
known as cross-shareholding. During the economic slump of the 1990s, they started to sell off these shares --
one factor that kept share prices falling for years. Stocks held as cross-shareholdings have dropped to below
20% of all shares on Japan's three main exchanges in terms of market capitalization, from more than 50% in
the early 1990s, according to Goldman Sachs.
As banks and business partners unloaded their shares, foreign investors picked them up and now own about
a quarter of all listed stocks in Japan. After shying away from the market for years, many domestic individual
investors have come back to the market in recent months, either through online discount brokers, mutual
funds or the country's fledgling retirement-savings system, which is similar to 401(k) plans in the U.S.
The Nikkei has enjoyed periodic rallies since falling from its peak of 38915.87 in December 1989. Those
rallies always fizzled out, and eventually the index reached a low of 7660.62 in April 2003, down 80% from its
peak.
Despite global investors' growing enthusiasm for the Tokyo market, Japan's institutional investors, even after
this year's rally, remain big sellers of Japanese stocks. Unable to shake off their deep skepticism after the
prolonged bear market, many corporate pension funds and insurance companies keep their domestic-stock
allocations at historically low levels and unload shares as soon as the market rises. In October, for example,
Japanese financial institutions, including insurers and custodian banks for pension assets, sold a net 1.1
trillion yen ($9.18 billion) of stock, roughly equal to the combined net purchases by foreign investors and
Japanese individuals.
Even without Japanese institutional investors, experts say a broader investor base will likely reduce the huge
volatility in the Japanese stock market, which is often compared with an emerging market rather than a

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
mature market in a developed economy. What's more, these new investors, along with newly aggressive
Japanese pension funds, are beginning to speak up and demand more from companies.
The unwinding of cross-shareholdings has led to the creation of "shareholder capitalism" in Japan, where
corporate management must respond to shareholders' demands for things like higher dividends and share
buybacks, says Kathy Matsui, Goldman Sachs's chief strategist in Japan. She expects Japanese stock prices
to rise an additional 15% next year.
Many Japanese companies have responded to shareholder issues. Companies such as Honda Motor Co.
and Mitsubishi Heavy Industries Ltd. trimmed the size of their boards this year to make decision-making more
efficient. And 41% of companies surveyed by the Tokyo Stock Exchange this year said they now have at
least one director appointed from outside the company, compared with 28% of respondents two years ago.
Disclosure also is improving. Most companies now report earnings quarterly, rather than semiannually, and
focus on consolidated earnings, rather than more limited parent-only results. Meanwhile, more annual
shareholder meetings are scheduled to allow for better attendance, and proxy materials are sent out earlier to
give investors more time to respond.
Still, says Federated's Mr. Auth, Japanese managements tend to be less forthcoming than their U.S.
counterparts in discussions with investors. "There are still plenty of companies that won't give you as much
disclosure as U.S. companies do," he says. But it is encouraging, he adds, that while in the past only giant
exporters like Toyota Motor made the effort to open up more with foreign shareholders, now "some of the
smaller, domestic-oriented companies are getting better, too."
The Tokyo exchange itself has become tougher as a watchdog for listed companies. For example, it now
requires top managers at listed companies to submit written oaths on financial statements and soon will ask
companies to file standardized corporate-governance reports. In a move that surprised the market, the TSE in
May delisted Kanebo Ltd. after learning that the cosmetics maker had been overstating its profits for years.
"We want to have an economy that can fight competitively on the global stage," says Eisuke Nagatomo, a
managing director of the TSE. "To do so, Japanese companies must speak up and explain who they are and
disclose and be accountable."
But not all of the overhaul steps are being implemented smoothly. The TSE recently proposed banning a
practice that allows privileged shareholders to veto strategic corporate decisions such as mergers and
acquisitions. But the proposal met with opposition from government officials and business leaders, and its
fate remains uncertain.
And smaller companies still "must convince investors that the board is committed to increasing shareholder
value and is not merely interested in protecting incumbent management at all costs," says Marc Goldstein,
representative director of the Japan office of Institutional Shareholder Services, which helps institutional
investors with proxy voting.
---
Other Market Activity
In addition to Japan's five-year mark, South Korean shares set a record for the fourth time in five sessions,
while most smaller Asian markets declined. European shares rose as the European Central Bank raised its
benchmark interest rate a quarter percentage point. The Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 1.03%, or 2.36,
to 230.63. Excluding the U.S., it rose 0.79%, or 1.49, to 189.60.
In SEOUL, the Korea Composite Stock Price Index, or Kospi, rose 8.54 points, or 0.7%, to 1305.98. Tech
shares led the rally, with Samsung Electronics and LG Electronics gaining after lowering prices on LCD TVs.
Hyundai Heavy Industries rose 2.1% after an order from Cuba. Shipbuilders fell on profit-taking, with
Samsung Heavy Industries down 1.3%.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 index rose 62.90 points, or 1.2%, to 5486.10. HSBC's strong results helped to lift
banking issues. Steelmaker Corus Group added 6.3% after a Morgan Stanley upgrade. Pub operator Punch
Taverns rose 3.7% after it agreed to buy Spirit Group.

Nikkei, Gold Hit Milestones; Dow Nears One --- Precious Metal Tops $500 Mark, And DJIA Adds 106.70 Points To Close In on Key 11000 Level
Thursday's Markets
Nikkei, Gold Hit Milestones; Dow Nears One --- Precious Metal Tops $500 Mark, And DJIA Adds 106.70
Points To Close In on Key 11000 Level
By E.S. Browning
503 words
2 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IN A DAY OF ACTIVE trading across the financial markets, gold surpassed $500 an ounce, the Nasdaq
Composite Index jumped to a 4 1/2-year high and the Dow Jones Industrial Average surged 106.70 points.
The stock rally began with Tokyo's Nikkei index surpassing 15000 for the first time in five years. U.S. stock
futures rallied before regular hours as the European Central Bank raised its benchmark interest rate to 2
1/4%. Higher rates could create headwinds for European companies. With the Federal Reserve expected to
stop raising U.S. rates next year, analysts recommended a shift toward U.S. stocks.
Then, a government report showed a consumer spending-based inflation gauge, said to be a
Federal-Reserve favorite, was unexpectedly tame in October. That quieted the inflation fears that had
knocked down stocks earlier in the week.
So after selling to cash in gains as November ended, investors went on a buying binge.
The Dow's gain of almost 1% left the average at 10912.57, up 1.2% for the year but still a tad short of the 4
1/2-year high of 10940.55 reached in March. Talk again spread among investors that the industrials might
push through the 11000 barrier for the first time since 2001.
Some short-term investors had been selling as "Dow 11000" neared, betting that stocks would pause ahead
of the big number. But November, December and January typically form the year's strongest three-month
period, and many investors began betting the pattern would repeat.
"Days like this in my opinion are indicative of people trying to get ahead of" expected year-end gains. "I think
it is going to carry higher," said Todd Clark, director of stock trading at San Francisco brokerage and
money-management firm Nollenberger Capital Partners.
The Nasdaq Composite Index jumped 1.54%, or 34.35 points, to 2267.17, a 4.2% gain for the year and the
highest finish since before the 2001 terrorist attacks. The S&P 500-stock index rose 1.22%, or 15.19 points,
to 1264.67, up 4.4% for 2005 but just short of a 4 1/2-year high.
Amid rising demand, gold surged 1.6%, or $7.90, to $502.50 in New York trading -- its highest finish in
almost 23 years. Typically, gold rises amid fears of inflation, dollar weakness, political trouble or economic
disruption. But this time, it jumped despite a quieting of those fears.
There was some negative news. Treasury-bond prices fell, pushing yields higher, reflecting continuing
inflation fears among bond investors. Inflation fears force the Fed to push interest rates higher, making
existing bonds less attractive.

Stock Index to Close Above 15000" -- WSJ Dec. 2, 2005)

Beyond the Bubble
Beyond the Bubble
By Michael T. Darda
1,007 words
1 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
An increasing number of economists and strategists on Wall Street expect the recent cooling in home sales to
turn into an outright freeze in the residential real estate market. Data showing that median home prices of
existing one-family homes rose 16.6% in October from a year earlier, the fastest pace since 1979, only
reinforced the view that an unsustainable boom is likely to end in tears. The working hypothesis is that the
undertow from a softening residential real-estate sector will eventually weaken national home prices and
pressure household asset values. The reverse wealth effect that would ensue is expected to cut into
consumer spending and severely weaken -- if not end -- the current U.S. economic expansion.
But despite the dire warnings to the contrary, a deceleration in national home price appreciation (which is
likely) will not necessarily lead to a slump in nationwide home prices or the negative externalities associated
with it. While certain regional real-estate markets could indeed be out of whack with the fundamentals -- and
thus experience price declines -- this does not appear to be a nationwide phenomenon.
Home prices have been rising faster than incomes, but the ratio between average home prices and incomes
remains below historical averages. Similarly, the ratio between total capitalization of household real estate
relative to capitalized incomes (i.e., incomes adjusted for long-term interest rates) also remains below
historical averages. Of course, this is no guarantee that home prices will continue to appreciate rapidly. It
does suggest, however, that a broad-based decline in median or average home prices, which would be a
threat to the growth outlook, remains unlikely.
The two-decade decline in homeowner equity as a fraction of household real estate continues to be a major
concern, but the Fed's Flow of Funds data shows that homeowner equity as a fraction of household real
estate stood at 56.8% during the second quarter. This is essentially in line with the 57% ratio that prevailed
during 1997, a year in which a drop in the tax rate on capital gains (including up to a $500,000 homeowner
exemption), helped to ignite the real estate boom in the first place.
While some contend that the boom in residential real estate has pushed real home prices to record -- and
unsustainable -- levels, this depends on the definition of real. If we use gold (which is real) as a deflator
instead of the Consumer Price Index (which is not), the median home price stood at $216,200 as of October
2005, compared with $334,000 (in 2005 gold-based dollars) in July 1970. In other words, while there might
be froth around the edges, median home prices have yet to fully catch up with the decline in the value of the
dollar since the U.S. left the last vestiges of the gold standard more than three decades ago.
Unfortunately, the ongoing debate over the existence of a house price bubble and its likely aftermath has
pushed the news of an economic acceleration out of the headlines. Business equipment production rose
6.5% during October, more than offsetting the 4.6% decline during September, when the effects of two major
hurricanes and the Boeing strike slashed output. High-tech production, which hasn't missed a beat, is up
24.8% year-over-year, the fastest annual rate of increase since the tail end of the tech boom.
Backlogs for non-defense capital goods excluding aircraft and parts, a gauge of future capital spending,
accelerated to the fastest three-month annualized pace since mid-2004 in the three months ending in
October. The same behavior is occurring in measures of core retail sales (sales excluding autos and
gasoline). They rose at the fastest three-month annual pace since early 2004 during the same period. These
are signs of an acceleration, not symptoms of a slowdown.
The news on the corporate sector is even better. According to Standard &Poor's, S&P 500 operating earnings
have compounded at double-digit year-over-year rates for 13 consecutive quarters. After taking a nosedive
during the profitless prosperity of the late 1990s and early 2000s, corporate pricing power is back. Unit prices
in the non-farm business sector advanced 2.9% year-over-year during the third quarter, the fastest annual
increase since 1991. In short, corporations have both profits and pricing power, which means they have the
resources to continue to spend. Since non-residential fixed investment makes up nearly two-thirds of gross

--- Page 32 ---
Page 32 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
domestic investment, these trends should work to offset any weakness that arises on the back of a slower
residential real estate market.
---
Yet despite the favorable disposition of many indicators of grassroots growth, there are real risks to the
outlook. If destructive tax hikes emerge from a failure to extend the 2003 tax cuts on capital gains and
dividends, asset prices would have to adjust lower to reflect lower expected after-tax returns. At the same
time, if the Fed eventually becomes overly restrictive with short rates and liquidity, the household and
corporate sectors could weaken in unison.
While the outlook for tax policy is up in the air, it is worth noting that dual weakness in the housing and
corporate sectors during previous decades typically followed in the footsteps of the Fed pushing short-term
interest rates five percentage points or more above the core inflation rate. This would require a 7% funds
rate at today's 2% rate of core inflation -- three full percentage points above today's Fed funds target rate of
4%. With liquidity-sensitive precious-metal prices surging to levels not seen in decades and real short rates
still below historical norms, the risk of near-term Fed overshoot is not compelling. Instead, an underestimation
of just how strong the economy is, and how easy the Fed has been, is likely to come into further focus during
the quarters ahead.
---
Mr. Darda is chief economist and director of research for MKM Partners.

It Keeps Going, and Going...
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
It Keeps Going, and Going...
772 words
1 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
We interrupt your daily doom-and-gloom programming with a word from the real economy: It's even better
than advertised. October's estimate of 3.8% third-quarter GDP growth was revised upward yesterday to 4.3%,
which means the expansion was moving fast enough in late summer to blow right past Hurricane Katrina.
This represents the fastest expansion since the first quarter of 2004, as well as the 10th consecutive quarter
of growth averaging close to 4% on an annual basis. So much for those predictions of recession we heard in
the spring, and again in September. In fact, has there ever been a U.S. expansion this robust that has been
accompanied by so much disbelief and predictions of imminent collapse? Not since the 1980s, we'd guess.
The third-quarter GDP revisions were especially notable for showing strength nearly across the board.
Durable-goods orders were particularly strong, increasing at an annual rate of 10.5% and up 6% from the
third quarter of 2004. Gross private investment grew at 5.8%, real equipment and software spending at nearly
12%. In other words, business investment has been a major growth driver, contrary to the conventional
wisdom that consumers have been sustaining growth only by emptying their "over-extended" wallets. This
bodes well for future growth, even as the housing market continues to cool.
If there was a third-quarter weak spot, it was exports, which grew at an annual rate of just 0.8%. But that says
more about the anemia of certain large markets overseas (read: Europe) than it does about the American
economy. Which makes it ironic that the Paris-based OECD made a point in its report this week to warn
about the "increasing" risks to the global economy caused by American "imbalances." This is one more
warning about the U.S. "twin deficits," in trade and the budget, neither of which is the world's largest
economic worry.
The trade deficit results in part from rapid U.S. growth sucking in goods from around the world. Without
exports to America and a fast-growing China, much of Europe would be moribund. As for budget deficits,
we've been harder than anyone on Congressional spendthrifts. But it'd be nice if the OECD conceded that the
Bush tax cuts of 2003 had something to do with the recent American, and global, boom. Those 10 quarters of
4% growth happened to begin almost precisely at the time it became clear tax cuts on dividends, capital gains
and income were going to pass the Congress.
None of which is to say that there aren't risks on the horizon. The biggest is inflation; the price index for
personal consumption, which is used to adjust the GDP numbers for inflation, was up 3.1% from the third
quarter of last year and was running at an annualized 3.6% in the third quarter, according to yesterday's
estimate. The price of gold also crossed above $500 an ounce Tuesday for the first time in 18 years, and
although it fell back to around $497 an ounce yesterday, it's still up substantially in the last year. We think this
gold boom represents an investor bet on future price increases.
The policy risk is that inflation will keep surprising markets on the high side, and thus the Federal Reserve
will have no choice but to keep tightening credit for longer (and with higher interest rates) than investors now
imagine. We're not smart enough to know how this will turn out, but Ben Bernanke and his Fed brethren
should be doing a lot of collar tugging as they watch price signals. They're the ones who created these
inflation expectations.
The other big danger is policy mistakes out of Washington. With President Bush's approval at low tide, all
sorts of bad ideas are on the loose -- from "windfall profits" taxes on oil companies to tariffs against China.
The Republican Congress is in such disarray that it hasn't been able to extend even for two years -- to 2010
from 2008 -- the 15% tax rates on capital gains and dividends that have contributed so much to this
expansion. The threat of their expiration will start to affect business and investment decisions well before the
clock strikes midnight on December 31, 2008, unless Congress acts.

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Those risks aside, last quarter's GDP numbers show that the U.S. economy can withstand natural disasters,
rising interest rates, $70 oil, $4 gasoline -- and the relentless pessimism of elite forecasters who said today's
prosperity could never happen.
