Page 37 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dow Industrials Decline Amid 11000 Jitters --- Big Round Number, Interest Rates Rattle Investors at Month's End; Was That the Christmas...
Wednesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Decline Amid 11000 Jitters --- Big Round Number, Interest Rates Rattle Investors at
Month's End; Was That the Christmas Rally?
By E.S. Browning
470 words
1 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
DESPITE A FRESH INFUSION of strong economic news, the Dow Jones Industrial Average tripped again in
its run toward 11000, finishing the day with a sharp decline.
Some traders blamed fear of the Federal Reserve, some blamed month-end profit-taking and some blamed
simple market jitters as the Dow neared a big, round number. Whatever the cause, the blue-chip average fell
82.29 points, or 0.76%, to 10805.87, now up just 0.2% this year. It held on to a gain of 3.5% for November,
the year's second-strongest monthly increase, after July's 3.6%.
"There's all this talk about a Christmas rally, but I think we may have seen it, from 10200 to 10900," said Todd
Leone, head of listed trading at brokerage firm SG Cowen. The Dow industrials may ultimately break 11000,
he said, but right now people are worried about the prospect of more Fed interest-rate increases.
The Fed's "beige book" report on the economy said some companies are raising prices due to higher energy
costs, although it didn't report significant wage increases. And the government's revised measure of
third-quarter economic growth was stronger than expected.
Those reports reinforced inflation fears, suggesting that the Fed will have to keep raising its target short-term
interest rates to keep the economy from overheating. Higher rates hold down spending by consumers and
businesses alike, which is bad for stocks.
Also weighing on stocks was a rise in crude-oil futures after a government report showed oil inventories
falling sharply last week.
The prospect of higher rates sent the dollar to a fresh two-year high against the yen.
Gold, which had neared a $500-an-ounce New York close earlier in the week, fell back to $494.60.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.64%, or eight points, to 1249.48, still up 3.1% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index was little changed, rising less than 0.01%, or 0.11 point, to 2232.82, up 2.6% for
2005.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed and bonds fell. On the Big Board, where 1.77 billion shares traded, 1,624 stocks rose and
1,721 fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield up to
4.500%. The 30-year bond was down 4/32 to yield 4.703%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 119.82 yen, up from 119.60, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.1791 from $1.1787.

Fiscal Policies Help Manila --- Peso's Rally May Stick Around; Stocks and Bonds Also Benefit
World Stock Markets
Fiscal Policies Help Manila --- Peso's Rally May Stick Around; Stocks and Bonds Also Benefit
By James Hookway
1,061 words
30 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THIS YEAR'S pre-Christmas rally in the Philippine peso could be more than the usual flash in the pan, some
economic analysts say.
The peso often gets a late-year boost from the seasonal remittances from the 10 million Filipinos overseas.
Money has also flowed into Philippine stocks, which are up 16% this year. Key fiscal policies are now in place
that may provide a platform for a sustained rise in the value of the peso against the U.S. dollar, analysts
argue.
Chief among them is an expanded value-added tax. The E-VAT law, as it is known, finally took effect at the
beginning of November after months of legal and political challenges. The law removed numerous VAT
exemptions on certain services and products, and the government expects it to raise about $70 million a
month in additional revenue.
The new tax legislation also lets President Gloria Macapagal Arroyo raise the rate of VAT to 12% from 10%.
While the date for implementing the higher tax rate has been pushed back to February from January, many
analysts are optimistic there won't be further delays. They and investors are encouraged by what the new
revenue stream will mean for the Philippines' financial stability. The government forecasts the higher tax, plus
an increase in corporate tax to 35% from 32%, next year will generate about 81.4 billion pesos, or around
$1.5 billion.
The seasonal influx of cash to this predominantly Christian country is playing a crucial part in the current rally
of the peso, which has climbed from a low for the year of 56.34 pesos to the dollar on Sept. 19, to 53.945
against the dollar in local trading yesterday. This year the central bank expects Filipinos working abroad to
send home a record $10.3 billion, up from $8.55 billion last year.
Stocks and bonds also have benefited. "Ever since it became clear that the E-VAT was going to be
implemented, there have been capital flows into Philippine bonds and some into the equity market, too,"
nudging up the peso's value in the process, says Luz Lorenzo, an economist at ATR-Kim Eng Securities in
Manila. "And these gains can be sustained because of the changes in the fiscal outlook."
A recent lowering in the volume of political noise from the Philippines also has helped. Ms. Arroyo survived
one impeachment attempt that opponents mounted after accusing her of trying to manipulate the outcome of
last year's presidential election. Her pledge to pave the way to change the Philippines' adversarial
presidential-style system into a parliamentary form of government also encourages some investors tired of
the incessant back-biting and plots characterizing the country's politics.
While the Philippines has benefited from global investors' efforts to search out the best yield -- the
attractiveness of its sovereign debt also has helped to support the peso in recent weeks -- its value in
comparison with other currencies is beginning to recede as interest rates begin to creep up across the
board. Simon Flint, a currency strategist at Merrill Lynch in Singapore, expects that while the peso will
continue to appreciate, "it will underperform the rest of Asia."
In short, he contends, better currency bets could emerge elsewhere in Asia. A bounce in demand for
electrical components and semiconductors, for example, will benefit other countries, not just the Philippines.
A global growth upswing also is encouraging governments from South Korea to Malaysia to look at raising
interest rates to contain inflation, a policy that could strengthen their currencies against the dollar. "The
Philippines doesn't look so special then," Mr. Flint says.
---
Yesterday's Market Activity

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
European markets gained behind importers, which benefited from a strengthening dollar, and mining issues.
Tokyo broke a string of eight consecutive positive sessions and dragged most of Asia lower. Overall, the Dow
Jones World Stock Index fell 0.07%, or 0.16, to 229.15. Excluding the U.S., the index fell 0.15%, or 0.29, to
188.61.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 index rose 13.60 points, or 0.3%, to 5491.00. Major mining stocks such as BHP
Billiton and Rio Tinto gained ground as precious-metals prices rose; smaller gold companies were mixed.
Financial stocks were pressured by banking group Barclays, which fell 1.1% on bad-debt concerns. Catering
company Compass Group rose 8% after pretax profit and sales beat analysts' expectations.
In TOKYO, the 225-share Nikkei Stock Average fell 59.24, or 0.4%, to 14927.70. Profit-taking hit blue-chip
stocks, with Advantest, Tokyo Electron, Toyota and Honda among companies giving back recent gains.
Softbank, the most active issue, was up as much as 4% before ending down 2.3%. Rakuten rose 7.9% after
stepping away from its merger proposal with TV broadcaster TBS, which fell 8.4%. Sumitomo Metal Mining,
Japan's largest gold producer, rose 2.6% as spot gold hit $500 for the first time since 1987.
In JOHANNESBURG, the All-Share Index fell 166.16, or 1%, to 17030.53 amid steady oil prices and a
stronger rand. The petrochemicals group's Sasol slid 3.5%. The rising rand even overcame high
precious-metal prices to hit miners. Anglo Platinum, the world's biggest platinum producer, shed 1.97%;
AngloGold Ashanti fell 2.11%. SABMiller fell 1% on talk that it might bid for Scottish &Newcastle.

Dow Industrials Flirt With 11000; Bond Prices Fall
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Flirt With 11000; Bond Prices Fall
By E.S. Browning
560 words
30 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average made a brief run at a 4 1/2-year high and pushed closer to the 11000
mark, but then lost steam early in the day as reports of economic strength sparked fears of more Federal
Reserve interest-rate increases.
After pushing as high as 10959.79 soon after trading began, the Dow industrials pulled back to finish with a
decline of 2.56 points, or 0.02%, at 10888.16, still up 1% this year. Other stock indexes also were little
changed.
Part of the day's problem was the interest-rate worry, but another big part was a classic case of market jitters
in the face of a significant milestone. After several weeks of gains -- as is the case now -- professional
investors often view an approaching milestone as a reason to step back, cash in some recent gains and wait
to see whether the market really has the oomph to push higher. This time, two milestones loom: the 4
1/2-year high of 10940.55 that dates from March, and the 11000 mark, which the Dow hasn't hit since 2001.
"We are up 600 or 700 points here in just a few weeks. November has been a great month for the market,"
said Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore investment-management group T. Rowe Price. "We are
hovering just under 11000 and it could take some time to get through it."
Gold, meanwhile, is bumping up against a milestone of its own -- the $500 mark. After pushing through $500
an ounce in overseas trading, gold also pulled back, finishing up 80 cents at $499.10 in New York.
Before stock trading began, investors learned of strong gains in October orders for durable goods -- things
like appliances and machinery. That spurred the early stock gains. Later in the morning came reports that
new-homes sales rose to a record in October and that the Conference Board's November
consumer-confidence reading was strong.
Those reports suggested that both consumers and businesses are still spending, which normally would be
good news for stocks. But the signs of economic strength sowed fears that the Fed might continue pushing
up interest rates longer than had been hoped, in order to keep the economy from overheating.
The prospect of higher interest rates helped the dollar, which resumed its rise, although it didn't quite return
to last week's highs.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index was almost unchanged, rising 0.02 point, less than 0.01%, to 1257.48,
still up 3.8% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.30%, or 6.66 points, to 2232.71, up 2.6% in 2005.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.60 billion shares traded, 1,958 stocks rose and 1,346 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 19/32, or $5.94 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.484%. The 30-year bond was down 1 4/32 to yield 4.696%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 119.60 yen, up from 118.79, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1787 from $1.1852.

Gold Pierces the $500 Barrier For First Time Since Late '80s
Commodities Report
Gold Pierces the $500 Barrier For First Time Since Late '80s
By Alison Guerriere Ciaccio Dow Jones Newswires
485 words
30 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold futures broke through the $500-an-ounce barrier for the first time in nearly two decades during Asian
trading yesterday, but gave back some of their gains to settle just under that level on the Comex division of
the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Front-month December gold by the end of the day had risen 80 cents to $499.10 an ounce. The contract
traded down to $495 during the New York session before popping to a $499.50 session high ahead of the
close.
Most of the action happened overnight in Asia, where Japanese investors showed "a particular appetite for
the metal," said analysts at MKS Finance SA in Geneva.
After reaching the $500 milestone, spot gold hit an intraday high of $502.80 an ounce during the Asian
trading session -- marking its highest level since late 1987.
Gold has rallied in recent days on the heels of investment demand along with support from comments from
Russia's central bank chairman that it will increase gold stocks in a bid to diversify foreign reserves, but
MKS analysts said fears of a "needed correction" likely were behind the cautiousness in the New York
market.
Bill O'Neill, one of the principals at Logic Advisors, said gains in the U.S. dollar left gold hesitant to test the
$500 level during the day session in New York.
Despite the slight decline late Tuesday, Mr. O'Neill said the market remains "in good shape."
"There is strong interest from the speculative community and gold is now being viewed as a currency," said
Mr. O'Neill, who added that demand out of the Middle East is also underpinning the market.
While the rally that pushed gold above $500 in late 1987 was seen as short-lived, market players contend
that this rally is more sustainable. George Gero, senior vice president at Legg Mason Wood Walker Inc., said
gold will remain strong but will see a "tug-of-war" on both sides of the $500 level.
"It's more sustainable now than 18 years ago because people are looking at interest rates, currency
fluctuation and are concerned about purchasing power," said Mr. Gero.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures on the Nymex sank in the wake of sharp losses in gasoline and heating oil prices.
Increasing U.S. petroleum supplies and warmer-than-usual weather have eroded concerns about supply
falling short. The January contract fell 86 cents to $56.50 a barrel.
WHEAT: Prices at the Kansas City Board of Trade rose sharply, supported by reports that Iraq tendered for
shipping in December 250,000 metric tons of U.S. wheat and hopes for the sale of 200,000 additional metric
tons to Iraq near term. December KCBT wheat rose 11.75 cents to $3.5925 a bushel.

This May Be as Good as It Gets
Global View
This May Be as Good as It Gets
By George Melloan
1,072 words
29 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
My neighbors Tom and Carol Phelan set out at 7 a.m. Friday morning in search of post-Thanksgiving
bargains, only to return empty-handed. The parking lots at the malls were already jammed. Early birds had
pounced on the low-cost treasures supplied by the People's Republic of China for America's Christmas joy.
Nationwide, retail dollar volume was well short of spectacular, probably because of the intense price
competition. But with the economy growing at a 3.6% annual clip and 142 million Americans (62.9% of the
adult population) holding jobs, it looks as if Christmas trees will be surrounded with an ample number of gifts.
The dollar is strong, the stock market is up, the UPS trucks are busy -- the U.S. prospers.
But of course it is the unhappy task of economists to look for whatever clouds might float over this rosy
scenario. They can be found if you search diligently enough. The warning signs require some interpretation.
The biggest worry at a time of booming consumer demand is inflation. The Consumer Price Index, the
inflation index that holds the most meaning for the average householder, was up 4.3% in October from a
year earlier. That was despite some softening of fuel prices that probably continued through November.
When price-level growth nears 5%, it is no longer negligible. At the estimated inflation rate for 2005, a dollar
would lose a quarter of its value in 10 years.
To be sure, the dollar has been strengthening relative to the Japanese yen and the euro, no doubt because of
the monthly interest-rate increases by the Federal Reserve this year. That probably accounts in part for the
stronger stock market; arbitrageurs see a reduced downside currency risk in investing in dollar securities.
But the gold price and commodity indexes are still rising against the dollar, suggesting that in fundamental
terms, the dollar has yet to return to a convincing level of muscularity.
The Fed's upward interest-rate ratcheting has had one other significant result. It has let a little air out of the
housing bubble. Lombard Street Research reports that median new-home prices, which were shooting up at
double-digit annual rates through much of last year, are no longer rising. Lombard says that the easy credit
generated by the Fed last year fed the housing bubble, even as the CPI remained fairly stable.
Up until this year's tightening cycle, the Fed for months had been maintaining negative real interest rates,
meaning that the federal funds rate it controls was lower than the rate of inflation. After being positive for
some months, the rate went negative again in September, mainly because of higher inflation.
Whenever a bubble starts to deflate, there is cause for worry, and that's what economists get paid to do. The
problem: Many householders in this nation of homeowners converted the equity created by rising home
prices into ready cash through the use of home-equity loans. This borrowing, and the consequent buildup of
consumer debt, has boosted America's buying spree. Chief Economist Paul Kasriel of Chicago's Northern
Trust Co. reports that households were spending at an annual rate of $531 billion more than after-tax
earnings in the third quarter of this year.
Home prices are falling in some areas that have seen the greatest asset inflation. If this trend becomes
general, many of the households that have splurged heavily through borrowing against home equity could
find themselves financially embarrassed when their bankers come around demanding more collateral. When
borrowers are embarrassed, bankers get red faces as well.
In short, the Fed in its eagerness to finance a recovery from the 2000 dot-com bust may have opened the
spigots too wide for too long. By mostly watching out for consumer-price inflation, the Fed governors were
quite likely paying too little attention to the dangers inherent in asset inflation. Consumer prices were being
held down by massive imports from that great well of low-cost merchandise, China. All that liquidity being
generated by the Fed thus flowed heavily into real estate to fuel both a home-construction boom (good) and a

--- Page 49 ---
Page 49 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
home-price bubble (bad). Bubbles look good at first, too, as home owners enjoy the illusion of rising wealth,
but they are unpleasant when they finally deflate. Ask the Japanese.
A further effect of the illusion of inflation-free easy money could be seen in Washington, D.C. With interest
rates low, the Republican-controlled Congress spent money like it was going out of style. The result was a
massive buildup of government-generated demand, which will now contribute to inflationary pressures. The
huge pile of debt the Republicans rebuilt will also cost more to service if long-term interest rates rise further,
as they are likely to do.
Given the fact that the Chinese and Japanese are financing a large chunk of that debt, Congress had better
hope that American consumers continue buying Asian goods. Otherwise, the financing costs in the federal
budget could start shooting up just when a downswing in the business cycle is lowering federal revenues. A
drop-off in U.S. consumer demand won't be a happy event for the Chinese either, since the economic
development that has helped Beijing maintain at least a degree of social and political order is directly
dependent on the number of consumers passing through the checkout stalls at Wal-Mart.
Well, maybe the dangers are overblown. Quite possibly the deflation of the housing bubble will be gradual
enough so that present values won't decline sharply. If those values hold up, even if they are no longer rising,
most home-equity loans will stay above water. There may be some slowdown in consumer demand, but keep
in mind that the work force is almost fully employed and wages and salaries can still sustain significant
consumption. The U.S. still is jam-packed with creative entrepreneurs who adapt quickly to changing
business conditions. Finally, householders are pretty good, on average, at managing their balance sheets --
certainly far better than governments and even a great many corporations -- and a lot of those home equity
loans go to improve the real value of dwellings.
But in good times it is always worthwhile to consider ways it could all turn sour. Enjoy the Christmas season.

The Go-Go Greenback
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Go-Go Greenback
884 words
28 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Foreign-exchange traders, like star-struck lovers, are easily blindsided. So when most economists predicted
the decline of the U.S. dollar this year, pointing to expanding trade deficits, Wall Street expected doom and
gloom for the world's most handsome currency and hailed Asia's hot contenders. The problem is,
foreign-exchange rates often have little to do with trade figures, and the dollar rose. All hail the go-go
greenback, we say, for at least a while longer.
It's been a big year for the buck. So far this year, the dollar has risen roughly 13% versus the euro and the
Japanese yen. As for the biggest tease in 2005 -- that the dollar would tumble 5% to 10% versus China's
domestic currency, the yuan -- well, that hasn't materialized either. Preferring stability for its fledgling
domestic industries, Beijing has let the yuan rise less than 1% versus the dollar since it tinkered with its
exchange-rate regime in July. But that's another story.
The dollar remains attractive to investors for a few reasons, starting with interest rates. The Federal Reserve
has raised the price of credit for 12 consecutive meetings, cranking up short rates to 4%. Meanwhile, the
European Central Bank, supplying euros to a sluggish Continent, is only now thinking of budging from its
historic low of 2%, set in June 2003. If the dollar yields twice as much as the euro, that's an easy suitor to pick
from the crowd.
That explanation has currency in Asia, too, if you'll excuse our pun. Take the recent, dramatic greenback
appreciation versus the Japanese yen. At first glance, that's a bit counterintuitive, given that Japan Inc.
seems (finally) to be getting on its feet again. Corporate profits are healthy, household incomes are rising,
and banks' bad loans are down to manageable levels. Equity investors are plowing in; the Nikkei has been
hitting five-year highs.
This resembles what happened in the U.S. last year. The difference is that the Bank of Japan has made
noises that it will keep short-term interest rates at "extremely low levels." Rates already lag the Fed by a
wide margin, and that's set to continue for a while. What's more, unlike most of Asia, domestic Japanese
investors can put their money wherever they like -- and they're mostly buying foreign bonds. That's helping to
depress the yen, too.
This will have knock-on effects around Asia and help to keep the dollar strong. Japan is a big exporting
nation. If the yen doesn't rise, other export-dependent countries around the region won't want to disadvantage
their local industries by letting their currencies rise. Consider what's happened in little Singapore, where the
value of exports is an incredible 150% of its domestic output. The Singapore dollar, heavily managed by the
Monetary Authority in the city-state, has risen only 3% this year. Guess why.
This brings us to a simple point that's usually lost on Wall Street: At the end of the day, a currency isn't a true
commodity, like gold or wheat. It is a store of value. Its supply is determined by a central bank, which has a
monopoly on its creation. Foreign-exchange markets are dominated by a cartel of central banks, and
currency rates are a function of those central banks' monetary policies.
Nowhere is this truer than in Asia, where central banks happily attempt to manipulate foreign-exchange rates.
China, Malaysia, and Indonesia have all intervened heavily in the foreign-exchange market this year. (A
notable exception has been South Korea. The won has responded by holding steady against the greenback
this year, helping to contain inflation.)
The relative economic health of nations of course also influences currency demand. The U.S. economy has
continued to power along while most of Asia is gradually slowing and Europe bumps along. Most economists
expect America to expand at around a 3.5% rate in the fourth quarter and into next year -- nothing to sniff at.
Employment is rising. So far consumer spending remains strong, despite the slowdown in housing. This isn't
a picture of a country in distress.

--- Page 51 ---
Page 51 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Contrary to popular wisdom, all this has happened while the U.S. current account, a measure of commercial
trade in the balance of payments, hit a record $66.10 billion in September. The U.S.-Sino trade deficit to
October alone was $80.4 billion. Some economists are now predicting deflationary pressure on the yuan.
That's right: Wall Street was predicting the Chinese currency would rise only a few months ago, and now an
opposite trend may be emerging. That shows how fickle foreign-exchange markets can be.
It also shows how politicians venture into this area at their peril when they get excited about the effects of
shifting exchange rates on national "competitiveness." The overall economic causes and effects that derive
from a currency's relative value are by no means as easily discerned as protectionists like to pretend, as
witness the number of countries that have benefited from muscular currencies. That seems to be happening
right now as the U.S. prospers with a dollar that has surprised the markets with its relative strength.

Your Money Matters (A Special Report): Alternative Investing --- Easing into foreign markets
Your Money Matters (A Special Report): Alternative Investing --- Easing into foreign markets
By Gregory Zuckerman
1,172 words
28 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
R9
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In this regular column, we answer questions about alternative investments. Send them to reports@wsj.com.
Q: Are there alternative ways to invest overseas beyond foreign stocks and mutual funds?
A: With the U.S. stock market having a challenging year, and opportunities in foreign economies looking
brighter, more investors are shifting to overseas markets.
Certainly, the results have been more rewarding. The DJ Stoxx 50 Index, which tracks 50 blue-chip European
companies, is up about 19% so far this year, while the Nikkei 225 Stock Average, which tracks the Japanese
stock market, is up roughly 29%.
But investing in foreign stocks can be difficult for individuals. Only some larger foreign companies issue
American depositary receipts, or ADRs -- receipts for securities that physically remain in a foreign country but
trade in the U.S. like other stocks. And many foreign companies disclose much less financial information than
do U.S. businesses, making it harder to assess them. Sophisticated investors generally visit customers and
suppliers of foreign companies, but that is beyond the means of most individuals.
That's why many advisers recommend that investors use exchange-traded funds, or ETFs, to invest in foreign
markets. ETFs, the hottest thing in alternative investments, are securities that track a basket of stocks or an
index, much like an index mutual fund. They provide diversification and an ability to track a specific market.
But unlike a mutual fund, which sets a price for its shares once a day, ETFs are traded throughout the day
like a stock, allowing an investor to get in and out while also using stop orders, or orders to buy or sell when a
price is reached, and limit orders, or orders to buy or sell a stock at or near a specified price.
Even more important, the average ETF has an annual expense ratio of about 0.4% of assets, about half the
expense ratio of index funds and less than one-third that of actively managed stock mutual funds, which must
pay for a staff of analysts and traders.
Even more important, the average ETF has an annual expense ratio of about 0.4% of assets, about half the
expense ratio of index funds and more than a third lower than actively managed stock mutual funds, which
must pay for a staff of analysts and traders.
The advantages of ETFs are magnified when it comes to foreign shares, many analysts say. "Most individual
investors have a tough time finding a good mutual-fund manager for domestic stocks, but it's even harder for
[mutual funds that target] overseas stocks," says Barry L. Ritholtz, chief market strategist at Maxim Group, a
brokerage firm in New York. "And it is difficult to get good insight into news, reports and analysis for individual
foreign-company names."
Mr. Ritholtz is a fan of iShares MSCI Korea Index Fund, an ETF that tracks the Korean market under the
symbol EWY. Even though the fund already is up about 40% this year, Mr. Ritholtz says South Korea will
continue to emerge as a major supplier to China, along with the U.S. and Japan, helping stocks in the country
rise further.
Other foreign ETFs that analysts say look attractive include iShares MSCI Japan Index Fund (symbol: EWJ)
and iShares MSCI Pacific ex-Japan Index Fund (EPP), which tracks Pacific Rim stocks other than Japanese
shares. Those funds have weakened a bit lately, potentially creating a more attractive entry point.
For investors eager to target riskier, though potentially more explosive, emerging markets, ETFs to consider
include: iShares MSCI Brazil Index (EWZ), which tracks Brazilian stocks; iShares Trust S&P Latin America 40
I (ILF), for big Latin American companies; and iShares MSCI Malaysia (free) Index Fund (EWM), for
Malaysian companies.

--- Page 53 ---
Page 53 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Q: Are there better ways to invest in gold beyond shares of gold companies or gold futures?
A: Gold has climbed about 10% so far this year, to roughly $490 an ounce, and some analysts say prices
could go higher if rising inflation in the U.S. and elsewhere boosts demand for gold, a traditional haven in
times of rising prices.
But many investors have been frustrated with traditional ways to invest in gold, among the most precious of
all the metals. For one thing, shares of gold miners -- an obvious way to play the bull market in gold --
sometimes do a poor job of closely tracking overall gold prices. That's because they can stumble in their
production efforts or run into other problems that can curtail profits.
Newmont Mining Corp., for example, which produces gold around the globe, is up only about 7% this year,
and is down about 1% in the past 12 months. Freeport-McMoRan Copper &Gold Inc. is another big gold
producer, but the company also is involved in copper and silver exploration, making it an imperfect way to bet
on gold. The stock is up more than 40% in the past year -- a great return but also a reminder that it isn't a
pure play on gold prices.
Using futures contracts to trade gold can also pose challenges for individuals. Futures -- contracts to buy or
sell gold at a fixed price at a future date -- are highly leveraged investments, meaning that an investor can
control a large block of gold with little cash. The leverage can allow an investor to put down a little and make
a lot on a move in gold -- but it also makes it possible to lose more than the original investment. Gold
futures also are quite volatile. And discount brokers generally don't offer online futures trading, forcing
investors who want to trade gold futures to open an account with an online futures-trading firm.
A better solution: using a gold ETF. One example is streetTRACKS Gold Shares (GLD), which was launched
last November by the World Gold Council and allows investors to own shares backed by actual gold bullion:
Each share represents one-tenth of an ounce of bullion. Another ETF that issues shares representing gold
interests is iShares COMEX Gold Trust (IAU).
But keep in mind that gold already has climbed in the past few years, and many advisers suggest that gold
should represent only 3% to 15% of an investment portfolio. Jim Melcher, president of Balestra Capital
Partners Ltd., a New York investment advisory firm and hedge-fund manager, says gold investments
generally reward investors only during political or economic crises, or in times of inflation, when major
currencies lose value.
But with overall inflation in September rising at the fastest pace in 25 years, gold should continue to do well,
says Mr. Melcher. "Investors should have a portion of their assets in gold at this time," he says, "though it's
not something you want to hold forever."
---
Mr. Zuckerman is a special writer in the Wall Street Journal's New York bureau. He can be reached at
gregory.zuckerman@wsj.com.

Dow, Up 1.5% in Week, Has 11000 in Its Sights
Friday's Markets
Dow, Up 1.5% in Week, Has 11000 in Its Sights
By Peter A. McKay
493 words
26 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average rallied for the sixth straight day, as investors await signs whether
economic growth, the housing market and Christmas shopping are robust.
The Dow is in striking distance of the psychologically important 11000 level. In shortened trading Friday after
the Thanksgiving holiday, the Dow gained 15.53 points, or 0.1%, to 10931.62, up 1.5% on the week and less
than nine points away from a 4 1/2-year high.
Other indexes hit 4 1/2-year peaks on Wednesday, and extended their gains yesterday. The Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index on Friday rose 0.2%, or 2.64 points, to 1268.25, up 1.6% on the week. The Nasdaq
Composite Index rose 0.1%, or 3.03 points, to 2263.01, up 1.6% on the week.
Trading in oil and metals contracts was closed at the New York Mercantile Exchange, an extension of that
market's Thanksgiving holiday. But those commodities will be closely watched in the weeks ahead for
potential signs of trouble.
For the week, gold futures gained $6.50, or 1.3%, to $492.10 a troy ounce on Nymex's Comex division.
Traders say the metal, which is usually considered an investor haven during uncertain times, is flirting with
$500 a troy ounce for the first time since 1987 for a mix of reasons, including lingering inflation fears and
strong jewelry demand.
Crude oil gained 4.6% on the week, or $2.57, to $58.71 a barrel, boosted by cold weather in the Northeast
and demand for fuel to heat homes.
Strategist Jack Caffrey of J.P. Morgan Private Bank said the energy rally hasn't yet caused widespread fears
of a trickle-down effect on consumers. But he added that analysts will watch closely in the weeks ahead for
any sign that heating costs have become such a burden on consumers that they curb spending on other
items.
"The challenge is that we're not really going to get a better view until tonight or tomorrow," when retailers put
out data regarding their post-Thanksgiving sales, Mr. Caffrey said.
Retailing stocks were lackluster yesterday, a big holiday shopping day, but have been on a tear over the
longer haul. Wal-Mart Stores, a Dow component, fell 0.2%, or 8 cents, to $50.49 a share.
In the coming week, markets will digest fresh government reports on home sales, gross domestic product,
and unemployment.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note gained 10/32, or $3.125 per $1,000 in face value, pushing
the yield down to 4.431%. The 30-year bond rose 23/32 to yield 4.66%.
The dollar rose, to 119.62 yen, up from Thursday's 118.93; the euro fell to $1.1726 from $1.1788.

Dow Gains 51.15 as Fed Minutes Hint At Possible End to Rate-Increase Cycle
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Gains 51.15 as Fed Minutes Hint At Possible End to Rate-Increase Cycle
By Aaron Lucchetti
502 words
23 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER READING the latest Federal Reserve meeting minutes, investors decided there will be no place like
stocks for the holidays.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose for a fourth consecutive trading day, adding 51.15 points, or 0.47%,
to 10871.43, its highest closing level since March 8 and about 70 points shy of the 4 1/2-year high it set
March 4. The Nasdaq Composite Index and the S&P 500 also rose, pushing those benchmarks to fresh 4
1/2-year peaks.
Stocks traded listlessly until about 2 p.m. Eastern time, when the Fed released the notes from its Nov. 1
meeting. While the eight-page summary touched on continued inflation fears, investors focused on a
passage suggesting the Fed might stop increasing its target short-term interest rate soon.
Gerald Lucas, a bond strategist at Banc of America Securities in New York, pointed to a line in the minutes
saying the Fed "before long" would have to alter the way it communicates its policy and outlook. "It's a major
language change," Mr. Lucas said.
The bond market also read the statement that way, with prices of existing bonds rising and futures predicting
Fed moves would stop after two more rate increases, rather than more. An end to rate increases could help
stocks by making short-term investments with yields pegged to the Fed's rate less attractive. It could also
ease fears that businesses and others would have to sharply curtail borrowing and spending.
Rising rates have been "a source of friction," said Chip Dickson, chief U.S. strategist for Lehman Brothers.
Stocks enjoyed the tailwind of an improved holiday sales forecast from the National Retail Federation, but
were held in check by rising crude-oil prices amid expectations for chilly weather in the Northeast.
The minutes showed that the Fed's Open Market Committee remains concerned about inflation. The
November gold futures contract, used as a hedge against higher inflation, jumped again to an 18-year high
of $492.60 an ounce.
But the prospects for stabilizing rates "will get people to feel a little more optimistic," predicted Richard
Jandrain, a portfolio manager at Fort Washington Investment Advisers.
The Dow has rallied about 4.1% this month, putting the blue-chip index up 0.82% for the year. The S&P
yesterday rose 6.38 points, or 0.51%, to 1261.23, while the tech-laden Nasdaq advanced 11.89, or 0.53%, to
2253.56.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note rose 10/32, or $3.12 for each $1,000 invested, pushing
the yield down to 4.43%. The 30-year bond was up 5/32 to yield 4.657%.
The dollar weakened. It slipped to 118.82 yen from 118.93 yen; the euro rose against the dollar to $1.1812
from $1.1727.

Rare Religious Texts Draw Bids as Investments
Personal Finance
Rare Religious Texts Draw Bids as Investments
By Colleen DeBaise Dow Jones Newswires
545 words
23 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Collectors are increasingly pouring large sums of cash into rare 1611 King James Bibles, centuries-old
Matthew-Tyndale Bible leaves, Hebrew scrolls, prayer books and other ancient liturgical texts.
At Sotheby's Western Manuscripts sale in London in June, a three-volume, 13th-century Bible in Latin with
prologues attributed to St. Jerome sold for $1.8 million, while an 11th-century Bible sold for $164,081, well
above the estimate.
Meanwhile, one big online U.S. dealer, Greatsite.com, is gearing up for the busy Christmas buying season.
The Lake Mary, Fla., firm expects to clear $250,000 a month in sales of rare Bibles, continuing a growth trend
that began shortly after the stock market tumbled in 2000. "The interest in these early religious works is
definitely escalating," says Victor Gulotta, a Newton, Mass., bibliophile who has collected rare books and
manuscripts for decades.
Collectors who buy and sell Bibles say prices have been climbing. That's chiefly because rare biblical work "is
like California coastline real estate -- there's a finite quantity of it," says John L. Jeffcoat, Greatsite's owner,
who estimates that in recent years the value of most Bibles has appreciated by 15% annually, with first
editions rising as much as 25%.
Bibles, like other collectibles such as art, gold coins, gemstones and baseball cards, are considered "hard
assets," a class of investments popular with those seeking a safe haven from the stock and bond markets.
Other hard assets include real estate, metals and natural resources such as oil, gas and timber.
The chief appeal of any hard asset is diversification, as its performance typically is unrelated to the price of
stocks and bonds. Among the drawbacks is that such assets can be illiquid, or difficult to sell; prices can be
volatile; and unlike stocks, they have special storage and handling requirements. "The biggest concern could
be the hassle of protecting it," says Mark Ferris, an Old Saybrook, Conn., financial planner. "If you really had
a Gutenberg Bible -- could you keep it in your house on a stand?"
He recommends that people buy rare Bibles only if they enjoy collecting them, not for investment purposes,
and only when retirement savings are secure. Otherwise, "that's pretty risky," he says.
Investing wisely requires some expertise, not to mention a close inspection. A Bible whose binding is torn or
has scriptures missing is worth considerably less than one in mint condition. And investors need to keep in
mind that the Bible is the most printed book in the word. "Are old Bibles valuable? Generally speaking, no --
they are as common as road gravel," says Mr. Jeffcoat of Greatsite, who advises clients to consider Bibles
printed in North America in the 1700s, or printed in Europe in the 1500s or 1600s. "We are talking about
Bibles that are unspeakably rare."
Auction houses, private dealers and online sites sell rare Bibles and other religious works. The Antiquarian
Booksellers' Association of America, at www.abaa.org , posts a list of book fairs and a searchable database
of books and dealers. More information also can be found on the International Society of Bible Collectors'
Web site at www.biblecollectors.org .

PROPELLED BY optimism about the year-end outlook, the Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 53.95
points, pushing it back into positive territory for the year, for the first time since March.
Thanksgiving week historically is one of the market's strongest, as investors look ahead hopefully to the
Christmas shopping season and to the year-end stock rally that has characterized many years -- though not
all. This week began in keeping with tradition.
The Dow's 0.5% rise put it at 10820.28, the highest finish in eight months and about 120 points short of the 4
1/2-year high of 10940.55 hit March 4. The Dow is up 0.4% for 2005.

Reflecting the optimism about the economy, the 20-stock Dow Jones Transportation Average rose to another
record high, 4156.48.
Gold hit a fresh 18-year high on strong demand and inflation concerns, ending at $489 an ounce amid its
boosters' predictions it could cross $500.
Stocks were helped by news that the Conference Board's index of leading economic indicators -- measures
such as new orders that can presage the economy's direction -- rose in October for the first time in four
months, and that Boeing got orders for 26 new 787 jets. Investors shrugged off rising oil futures as colder
weather set in, and looked past General Motors' stock drop following its announcement of job cuts and plant
closures. Mentor's $2.2 billion bid for Medicis Pharmaceutical fueled a belief that executives consider many
stocks attractively priced.
The market gains appeared tied less to specific events than to a positive year-end view among money
managers who are hoping to finish the year with gains. "There's a lot of liquidity, interest rates are low,
corporate balance sheets are solid and investors are willing to take risks," said Jack Ablin, chief investment
officer at Harris Private Bank in Chicago.
The S&P 500 rose 0.53%, or 6.58 points, to 1254.85, up 3.5% this year. The Nasdaq rose 0.66%, or 14.60
points, to 2241.67, up 3% for 2005.

Trends (A Special Report): Securities Industry --- The Impact of Interest Rates
Trends (A Special Report): Securities Industry --- The Impact of Interest Rates
2,184 words
21 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
R8
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AS SURELY AS the movements of the moon and the sun rule the ocean tides, trends on Wall Street are
driven by the movement of the markets.
So with the Dow Jones Industrial Average having fallen in three of the past four years and about flat so far
this year compared with 2004, the securities industry is changing. Investors are looking for alternatives to
stocks to boost their returns, with some flocking to securities their parents never heard of. Meanwhile, as fees
and commissions have declined, Wall Street firms are finding other ways to make money. And stock
exchanges are consolidating as customers' expectations evolve along with trading technology. The regulatory
environment is changing, too.
But just as it can be difficult, if not impossible, to predict where financial markets are headed, there is risk and
uncertainty in many of these changes.
Here's a look at some of the trends on Wall Street.
1. BONDS TRUMP STOCKS
Ever since the dot-com bubble burst in March 2000, and the broader stock market tanked as well, financial
markets have been dominated by fixed-income securities -- in a word, bonds. And that has rearranged the
power structure and competitive landscape on Wall Street.
From March 31, 2000, through Sept. 30 of this year, the largest stock-index mutual fund, the Vanguard 500
Index Fund, fell an average of 2.1% a year, while the comparable bond-market fund, the Vanguard Total
Bond Market Index Fund, delivered an average annual return of 6.4%, according to fund tracker Morningstar
Inc.
Among the top securities firms, executives specializing in bonds have risen in the ranks, while some
stock-market veterans have had to pack their bags. And firms with the strongest bond-market franchises have
done better than others.
At Goldman Sachs Group Inc., a former bond executive, Lloyd Blankfein, became the firm's president and No.
2 executive in December 2003. At Morgan Stanley, fixed-income chief Zoe Cruz rose to No. 2 as well, while
two former stock-division chiefs, Vikram Pandit and John Havens, left the firm during the turmoil that
culminated with another bond-market veteran, John Mack, getting the top job in June 2005.
The rocket ride of bonds has been fueled by the series of interest-rate reductions the Federal Reserve made
to soften the blow to the economy when the stock-market bubble burst. But now that the Fed has reversed
course, raising its target interest rate 12 times since June 2004, the future is uncertain.
-- Randall Smith
2. MORE PRIVATE DEALS
Private-equity firms, flush with cash, are banding together to snap up companies in blockbuster deals, giving
them a growing percentage of global merger-and-acquisition activity. These buyout shops also are selling
their stakes faster than they did in the past, returning quick profits to investors and delivering huge fees to
Wall Street.
Investors -- mainly public and corporate pension funds -- have been putting more money into private equity as
an alternative to traditional stocks and bonds in hopes of boosting returns. Earlier this year, Carlyle Group
raised a fund in excess of $10 billion, and Warburg Pincus LLC raised an $8 billion fund.
Although buyout shops are taking in more and more money from investors, these firms still supply only a
fraction of an acquisition's value in cash; they make up the rest of the purchase price with debt. So to some

--- Page 67 ---
Page 67 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
extent, whether they can continue to pony up for big-ticket deals will rest on the affordability of borrowing. If
interest rates climb too high, it could take the wind out of deal activity.
-- Kara Scannell
3. MERGER FEVER
Companies around the world are taking the plunge on big acquisitions of competitors, helping to push the
total dollar volume of mergers and acquisitions this year beyond $2 trillion by late October, the highest level
since 2000.
France Telecom SA surprised rivals in July by buying Spanish mobile operator Amena. That helped spark
similar deals by other carriers, including the agreement by Telefonica SA of Spain to buy U.K. mobile
operator O2 PLC for GBP 17.7 billion ($30 billion), unveiled on Oct. 31.
The mining industry also continues to consolidate. Last month, Inco Ltd. agreed to acquire Falconbridge Ltd.
for $13.6 billion, which would create the world's largest zinc producer and a substantial player in coal, copper
and gold.
Next year is expected to be an active one for mergers and acquisitions, with shareholders becoming more
aggressive and companies continuing to put their cash hoards to work. An overall economic downturn could
damp the big spending, but a more likely scenario is that some highly leveraged private-equity deals will go
sour when the acquiring companies can't manage the debt payments, making both lenders and the chief
executives of companies considering acquisitions more cautious about pursuing new deals.
-- Dennis Berman and Jason Singer
4. STOCK EXCHANGES JOIN FORCES
A new wave of consolidation is under way among stock exchanges around the globe as the markets try to
keep up with customers' demands for fast, inexpensive trading of a wide range of stocks and derivatives like
options.
The New York Stock Exchange and the Nasdaq Stock Market earlier this year announced they would buy
fast-growing electronic competitors. The NYSE talks of future alliances with exchanges in Europe and Asia.
Overseas, markets have been merging across once insurmountable boundaries. Euronext NV -- formed in
2000 when bourses in Amsterdam, Brussels and Paris came together -- has explored the idea of mergers
with its peers in Frankfurt and London.
Customers applaud the idea of bigger exchanges that can cut costs through economies of scale, but worry
that the new behemoths may innovate less and wield oligopolistic pricing power.
Many exchanges have recently converted to for-profit corporations, adding pressure to grow bigger and more
profitable. Some stock exchanges and stock-options exchanges have merged, with many industry observers
predicting more such mergers to come. Futures exchanges could be a trickier partner for a stock exchange,
though, since futures are regulated not by the Securities and Exchange Commission, as stock markets are,
but by the Commodity Futures Trading Commission.
-- Aaron Lucchetti
5. REGULATION EVOLVES
Three years after a wave of financial fraud ushered in the Sarbanes-Oxley corporate-reform act, business
interests are pushing regulators to take a less aggressive approach.
While there's no indication that the toughened regulatory climate will disappear, regulators are listening to
complaints, and there are some signs that the environment may be easing.
The SEC has agreed to study a controversial -- and expensive -- rule mandated by Sarbanes-Oxley that
requires companies to assess controls they have in place to prevent accounting mistakes and fraud and have
their external auditors attest to those controls. The SEC recently delayed implementation of the rule for
companies with less than $75 million in market capitalization. New SEC Chairman Christopher Cox, in his first
public meeting since taking the job in August, proposed easing rules intended to speed up annual and
quarterly financial filings by public companies. Mr. Cox also has indicated that he wants to review some of the
recently adopted rules to make sure they're working and not having unintended consequences.

--- Page 68 ---
Page 68 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, one of the most aggressive securities cops, New York Attorney General Eliot Spitzer, may soon
be less focused on Wall Street. Mr. Spitzer, a Democrat, is running for governor of New York in 2006 and is
expected to spend more time campaigning and less time holding Wall Street's feet to the fire.
-- Deborah Solomon
6. ETFS TAKE OFF
Just five years ago, few investors had ever heard the term "exchange-traded fund." And that was for good
reason. There were only a handful of these mutual funds being offered, and they were the domain of big,
sophisticated investors.
Now ETFs, as they are known, hold $259 billion, much of which is money from small investors. These days
they're growing rapidly; assets in ETFs are up 15% this year, more than twice the pace of growth in traditional
funds. There are now 179 ETFs, designed to track a multitude of stock indexes, from the broadest measures
to the tiniest investing niches.
ETFs trade all day on an exchange like a stock, while a traditional mutual fund is priced just once a day. But
the real appeal to investors is their low cost: Investors can own them for a fraction of the cost of traditional
mutual funds.
So far, the ETF market has been dominated by two big players, Barclays Global Investors and State Street
Global Advisors. Vanguard Group, the best-known name in index funds, is a distant third but is beefing up its
efforts.
Even fund companies that don't offer ETFs are taking note. One reason Fidelity Investments slashed fees on
its index funds was to compete more effectively with ETFs.
-- Tom Lauricella
7. COMMODITIES ARE HOT
As the stock market has stagnated, investment banks, hedge funds and other firms have increased their
trading of natural resources, which generally have been rising in price for more than three years.
Brokerage firms Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley, for instance, have racked up big profits trading with
their own money in markets for crude oil and other energy products.
Recently, bidding was fierce to acquire the regulated commodities- and futures-trading unit of the bankrupt
brokerage firm Refco Inc. Stock in the Chicago Mercantile Exchange has soared nearly 11-fold since being
offered in late 2002, helped by record volumes in its markets for everything from livestock to futures contracts
on the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index. The rival Chicago Board of Trade followed suit with a public
offering of its own last month, and its share price already has more than doubled.
-- Peter A. McKay
8. HEDGE-FUND GROWTH SLOWS
Investors' love affair with hedge funds may be cooling.
Hedge funds today manage an estimated $1 trillion, up from just $400 billion five years ago. These loosely
regulated private partnerships held up well during the vicious bear market of 2000-02, and some hedge funds
have been able to outperform mutual funds and other competition by wide margins over the years.
But the industry's assets grew just 1.6% in the third quarter of this year, according to Tremont Capital
Management, which invests in hedge funds and tracks market data. Some investors have started to worry
that rising fees, and too many funds chasing the same ideas, are eating into returns. Most funds now charge
investors at least 1.5% of the assets invested and 20% or more of any investment gains.
Hedge funds have slightly outperformed the broad stock market so far this year, according to Tremont, but
last year their average 9.6% gain was below the almost 11% return on the S&P 500. A big problem: The
stock and bond markets have been remarkably placid lately and stocks aren't considered especially cheap or
expensive, making it harder for hedge funds to profit from strategies designed to take advantage of market
movements and anomalies.
-- Gregory Zuckerman
9. TRADING COMMISSIONS SHRINK

--- Page 69 ---
Page 69 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Trading is being increasingly automated and commoditized, and that's putting pressure on commissions.
In a new program with Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. that became public in October, mutual-fund giant
Fidelity Investments cut the amount it pays Lehman in trading commissions to well below the going rate of
five cents a share. The program allows Fidelity to pay separately for research it obtains from Lehman.
Historically, the costs of research were bundled into trading commissions, but Fidelity's move separating the
two and reducing the cost of commissions shows the squeeze on Wall Street.
Some firms were already bowing out of executing commission trades before Fidelity's move. In August, Wells
Fargo &Co. withdrew from stock trading for institutional investors. "The institutional equity business of Wall
Street is in trouble," wrote Brad Hintz, an analyst at Sanford Bernstein &Co., in August.
-- Randall Smith
10. RISKING THEIR OWN MONEY
A decade ago, the highest-profile job on Wall Street was offering advice on mergers and acquisitions, or
perhaps playing middleman on big stock transactions conducted by clients.
Today, the big money is made by so-called proprietary traders who use their firms' money to make big
wagers on stocks, bonds, commodities and currencies. For now, the securities firms have been getting it
right, in part because they say their risk controls have improved.
Goldman Sachs, for example, remains a leading adviser on global mergers and acquisitions. But analysts say
as much as 35% of the firm's revenue -- perhaps more -- comes from trading related to its own account, up
from approximately 25% three years ago. Other firms, like Morgan Stanley, also have been generating much
more money from this kind of trading.
An obvious problem with such activities is risk: A bad call on interest rates or the dollar can lead to big-time
losses. Skeptics worry that Wall Street is becoming too enamored with this risky business.
-- Gregory Zuckerman

Stocks Rise as Oil Falls, GM Revives, Stirring Fresh Talk of Year-End Rally
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Rise as Oil Falls, GM Revives, Stirring Fresh Talk of Year-End Rally
By E.S. Browning
493 words
18 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average broke out to an eight-month high, and the Nasdaq Composite Index hit
a four-year high, as falling oil prices and strength in General Motors and Google shares fueled fresh talk of a
year-end rally.
The Dow industrials, which had stalled below their summer highs, broke through to their best finish since
March 15, rising 45.46 points, or 0.43%, to 10720.22. They still are down 0.6% this year. The Dow Jones
Transportation Average rose 2% to a record 4082.39, up 7.5% this year.
Internet-related stocks rallied as Google pushed past $400 a share for the first time, more than doubling in
seven months. The Nasdaq composite, which includes Google, jumped 1.49%, or 32.53 points, to 2220.46,
its highest close since 2001. It is up 2.1% for 2005.
Gold rose $7.90 to $486.20 an ounce, an 18-year high, amid continuing talk that demand is outpacing supply
and that Russia's central bank plans to increase its gold reserves.
The stock gains began as crude-oil futures plunged $1.54 to $56.34 a barrel, a five-month low. Reports of
rising natural-gas inventories reassured investors about winter heating supplies.
Then GM, which had plunged 6% to a 14-year low Wednesday, recouped that loss on news Chief Executive
Rick Wagoner had told employees GM doesn't plan to seek bankruptcy-law protection.
"Oil was helping, and GM obviously was a big factor," said Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund
group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md. "Most people I talk to think this rally still has some legs."
Stocks got another lift after regular hours as Hewlett-Packard posted higher-than-expected period net and
sales, raising shares 5.8% after hours.
Housing starts fell more than expected last month, increasing concern the housing boom is fading, but
industrial production rose strongly.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index advanced 0.94%, or 11.59 points, to 1242.80. It is up 2.6% this year,
at a three-month high.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.19%, or 2.19 points, to 185.71.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the NYSE, where 1.69 billion shares traded, 2,440 stocks rose and 834 fell.
Bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.465%. The 30-year bond was up 10/32 to yield 4.651%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 118.72 yen, down from 119.12, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.1755 from $1.1674.

Crude Drops Below $57 a Barrel
Commodities Report
Crude Drops Below $57 a Barrel
By Leah McGrath Goodman Dow Jones Newswires
368 words
18 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude-oil futures dropped more than a dollar to settle above $56 a barrel, and marking a five-month low,
while gasoline futures fell to late-May levels in a surprise selloff.
The drop came as traders shifted their focus back to above-normal U.S. oil inventories and concerns about
softening demand, even as frosty weather crept into the Northeast and Midwest.
Benchmark light, sweet crude-oil futures contracts for December -- which expire Friday -- settled $1.54 lower
at $56.34 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The more actively traded Nymex January contract settled $1.30 lower at $57.15, but 2006 back months
touched $59 a barrel, signaling a bullish outlook.
Nymex December gasoline futures settled 2.35 cents lower at $1.4595 a gallon, while December heating-oil
futures settled at $1.6970 a gallon, down 3.22 cents.
Nymex natural-gas futures for December fell 38.7 cents to $11.942 per million British thermal units.
On the heels of a week's high in oil prices reached overnight, the downturn appeared to confirm the
vulnerability of energy market rallies, traders said.
Some saw the drop as further indication of a downtrend, but others weren't so sure.
"I think we're kind of getting to the bottom here," said Edward Meir, a commodities analyst for Man Financial
Inc., of New York. "I think the colder weather is going to be a constant and could provide some underlying
support."
Oil futures have fallen 20% since hitting a high of $70.85 a barrel on Aug. 30.
In other commodities markets:
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose as technical chart factors,
strong European and Asian purchases and inflation concerns combined to boost futures. The November
contract gained $7.90 to $486.20 an ounce. The most-active December contract rose $7.80 to $486.90.
COPPER: Comex futures climbed amid the continuing uncertainty surrounding a large short position on the
London Metal Exchange run up by a missing Chinese trader. The November contract rose 1.10 cents to
$2.0460 a pound.

Dollar, Bonds and Gold Rise Sharply; Stocks Stay Stuck Near Top of Range
Wednesday's Markets
Dollar, Bonds and Gold Rise Sharply; Stocks Stay Stuck Near Top of Range
By E.S. Browning
638 words
17 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCK INDEXES CONTINUED to stagnate near the top of the range where they have been stuck for most
of the past year -- on a day when the big action was in bonds, precious metals and currencies.
The dollar and Treasury bond prices rose sharply after a government report showed foreign purchases of
dollar-denominated securities hitting a record in September. Bonds also were helped by a government report
showing consumer prices rising only mildly in October. Low inflation usually keeps interest rates down,
making the yields on existing bonds more attractive.
The rising bond prices pushed down the yield of the benchmark 10-year Treasury note back below 4.5%, a
level it hadn't finished at since Oct. 24. Lower yields are good for stocks because they hold down market
interest rates, making it cheaper for businesses and consumers to borrow, but that didn't do much for the
stock market yesterday. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 11.68 points, or 0.11%, to 10674.76, down 1%
this year.
Gold futures rose $10.20 an ounce, or 2.2%, to $478.30, the highest New York close in nearly 18 years. It
was the biggest percentage gain in more than a year. Gold, considered a haven in turbulent times, was
reacting to continuing uncertainty about the future of stocks and bonds and to talk that gold miners are
having trouble keeping production high. It also was responding to a 25-year high for platinum. Other
commodities such as copper and silver also surpassed recent highs.
Stocks couldn't get going, despite the mild inflation report and an encouraging business outlook from home
builder D.R. Horton. D.R. Horton's forecast calmed some of the worries raised by a less upbeat projection
from Toll Brothers last week.
Some traders worry that the Dow, which is near the 10700 level where it topped out in the summer, could be
running out of steam again. But others said that, with the Dow up nearly 5% since late October, a pullback is
normal.
"Considering the rally that the market has had in the last two or three weeks, it is pretty impressive that stocks
have held on to so much of the gains," said stock trader Michael Driscoll at New York brokerage firm Bear
Stearns.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.18%, or 2.20 points, to 1231.21, up 1.6% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 0.05%, or 1.19 points, to 2187.93, up 0.6% in 2005.
The Dow industrials were held back by General Motors, a Dow component, which fell almost 6% to $21.29 a
share, its lowest finish since 1991, amid fears of a strike at parts-supplier Delphi, a former GM subsidiary.
GM's market value fell to just above $12 billion, by far the smallest among the 30 Dow components. Next
smallest is Alcoa, at nearly $23 billion.
American Express, another Dow component, also fell after its chief executive said some analysts' earnings
expectations were too high.
The dollar hit a new two-year high against the euro and the yen.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.58 billion shares traded, 1,599 stocks rose and 1,720 fell.
Bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 22/32, or $6.88 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.480%. The 30-year bond was up 1 9/32 to yield 4.669%.

What the In-Crowd Knows --- From Hollywood to Wall Street, Our Guide to the Blogs Insiders Read to Stay Current
What the In-Crowd Knows --- From Hollywood to Wall Street, Our Guide to the Blogs Insiders Read to
Stay Current
2,784 words
16 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
THE INTERNET BLOG site www.accountingobserver.com/blog/ is updated nearly every day. Some readers
get a weekly listing of the daily postings. A Personal Journal article yesterday incorrectly said the site is
updated roughly once a week.
(WSJ Nov. 17, 2005)
(END)
THE MUSIC INDUSTRY has one, Wall Street bankers have several and even CPAs have come around.
No self-respecting industry these days is without a must-read blog. Although they vary wildly on fine points
like accuracy, they are now so widely read that it's assumed anybody in the business is up to speed on the
latest postings. For outsiders, they are also a window into the inner workings, preoccupations and gossip of
fields ranging from real estate to mergers and acquisitions.
People who follow electronics got an early peek at a key new product when Engadget posted photos of
Microsoft Corp.'s XBox 360 videogame console a week ahead of its debut. TV executives keep tabs on which
networks are ordering and canceling shows. Doctors and others in health care can link to the latest news and
commentary on drug marketing. Reporters and media watchers turn to Jim Romenesko, who runs a blog on
the Poynter Institute's Web site.
There are three basic varieties of blogs: those that post links to other sources, those that compile news and
articles, and those that provide a forum for opinions and commentary. Some do one of these things or mix all
three.
Here are some of the most influential blogs across industries ranging from publishing and finance to health
care and Hollywood, put together by The Wall Street Journal's beat reporters in these areas.
Real Estate
-- Curbed.com
This blog attempts to deflate real-estate hype. Updated roughly a half dozen times a day, the site, run by
former magazine editor Lockhart Steele, takes on overpriced condo listings, pokes fun at the language
brokers use to pump up properties, and links to the relevant news from mainstream press and other blogs. A
running feature called PriceChopper highlights grossly overpriced apartments and takes credit when the
asking price drops. Another fixture called BubbleWatch links to optimistic market forecasts. Curbed's major
drawback is its New York-centric coverage and its obsession with celebrity and luxury properties. Recent
posts on projects in Los Angeles and Boston, however, indicate the site's willingness to acknowledge there is
a real estate world outside the Big Apple.
-- Alex Frangos
-- Theslatinreport.com
A sober alternative to Curbed, the Slatin Report delivers all-original commentary and analysis on the world of
commercial real estate. The brainchild of veteran real-estate journalist Peter Slatin, the site weighs in on
real-estate investment trusts, industry dealmakers, and design and architecture. A recent article pointed out
how Donald Trump could get locked out of profits for decades in a complicated transfer of a property he's
involved in. Mr. Trump disputed the characterization as "totally false." One issue with the site is that the posts
are sporadic and irregular. But users can sign up for an email alert when new items appear.

A popular opinion and news-analysis blog among traders and institutional investors written by Jeff Matthews,

Health Care
-- pharmamarketingblog.com
The site focuses on how drug companies can get accurate and trustworthy information to doctors and
consumers. John Mack, publisher of the monthly online newsletter Pharma Marketing News, started his blog
in January 2005. He offers commentary on news events and is often critical of the industry's focus on
blockbuster drugs and what Mr. Mack views as unethical or misguided marketing. Among his pet peeves is
erectile-dysfunction advertising, which he believes focuses too heavily on younger men and libido-enhancing
promises while failing to educate consumers about the disease. The site lambastes pharma companies for
ads that foster a "magic pill solution preference among Americans," while rarely mentioning changes in
lifestyle or diet that will help reduce risks such as cardiovascular disease. But he's quick to praise efforts that
address the industry's credibility problem with consumers, such as Johnson &Johnson's new TV and print
campaigns that he says put drug risks on more-equal footing with drug benefits.
