Dow Posts Small Loss on Investor Fears About Inflation, More Rate Increases
Thursday's Markets
Dow Posts Small Loss on Investor Fears About Inflation, More Rate Increases
By E.S. Browning
506 words
14 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS PUT A BRAKE on their recent losses, but big-stock indexes posted small declines as worries
spread about inflation and about more interest-rate increases.
Technology stocks rebounded slightly as Apple Computer surged following news of its new video-iPod and
entertainment-computer offerings.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average was virtually unchanged, falling 0.32 point to 10216.59, down 5.3% this
year and at a five-month low. Trading again was heavy, with 1.82 billion shares changing hands on the New
York Stock Exchange despite some investors' absence due to the Yom Kippur holiday.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, home to most technology stocks, rose 0.48%, or 9.75 points, to 2047.22,
down 5.9% for 2005. Apple jumped 9.1%, more than recovering the previous day's losses.
With the Dow industrials down more than 3% since the month began, investors stepped in to buy
beaten-down stocks. Several times during the day, the Dow pushed into positive territory, only to pull back
again.
One big problem for stocks was the Commerce Department's report on the trade deficit, which showed that
prices for imported goods rose 2.3% in August, the biggest increase in 15 years. That further stimulated
inflation fears, and fears that the Federal Reserve will raise short-term rates into next year in an effort to cool
the economy.
"The Fed has really spooked the market in the past two weeks," said Russ Koesterich, senior portfolio
manager at Barclays Global Investors in San Francisco. Recent comments from Fed officials, with Tuesday's
release of hawkish-sounding minutes from the Fed's September interest-rate meeting, stimulated
rate-increase fears.
Stocks were helped by falling oil and gasoline prices after an Energy Department report showing gasoline
consumption falling and refinery output rising last week. Crude prices fell $1.04 to $63.08 a barrel.
Gold, which had soared in October, pulled back, as some investors took profits. Gold futures fell $2.80, or
0.6%, to $471.10 an ounce.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.07%, or 0.84 point, to 1176.84. It is at a five-month low, down
2.9% this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 1.49%, or 2.75 points, to 181.39.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the New York Stock Exchange, 1,158 stocks rose and 2,153 fell.
Bond prices fell. The 10-year Treasury note was down 5/32, or $1.56 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.469%. The 30-year bond was down 17/32 to yield 4.697%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 114.32 yen, down from 114.39, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.2033 from $1.2029.

Chicago Merc Plans Expansion To Attract More Global Clients
Commodities Report
Chicago Merc Plans Expansion To Attract More Global Clients
By Andrew Batson Dow Jones Newswires
429 words
14 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Chicago Mercantile Exchange, the nation's largest futures exchange, said it is expanding its
infrastructure and products to cater to global clients, and expects that a quarter of its trading eventually will
come from overseas.
The Merc already has seven hubs -- communications platforms allowing a direct connection to the exchange
-- serving Europe, and in June it opened its first Asian hub in Singapore. This week, it launched a futures
contract based on 50 Asian stocks and plans other products that are more "relevant" to the interests of
international investors, a senior executive said.
"Generally speaking, anywhere from 12% and 18% of our volume currently comes from overseas funds," said
Phupinder Gill, president and chief operating officer of the exchange. "From what we see now, the number is
rising," he said in an interview, adding that an increase to 25% is "easily and rationally doable," although he
didn't give a time frame.
Trading volume at the exchange, a unit of Chicago Mercantile Exchange Holdings Inc., averaged 4.2 million
contracts a day in the third quarter, 30% more than a year earlier. Its main business is futures and options on
U.S. stock indexes and interest rates, though foreign-exchange and commodity-based products also are
traded.
China is of interest, Mr. Gill said, although regulations limit what the Chicago Merc can do. Futures trading in
China has been tightly restricted following scandals in the 1990s, and capital controls also limit the flow of
investment capital into or out of the country's securities markets.
The surge in trading volume has lifted revenue and earnings at the exchange, and contributed to a run-up in
the share price. But the scandal at Refco, a major Merc client, has hit the stock price. On the New York Stock
Exchange, shares of Chicago Merc were down $8.60, or 2.7%, at $310.40. The stock reached a record of
$346.50 a week ago.
In commodity markets yesterday:
CRUDE OIL: Futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange fell following U.S. government data showing a
robust recovery in oil-product output from the Gulf Coast amid deteriorating demand. The November contract
settled down $1.04 at $63.08 a barrel.
GOLD: Prices fell amid a decline in the value of the U.S. dollar versus the other major currencies, from
stronger levels earlier Thursday, and a drop in crude-oil futures prices. Gold for December delivery fell $2.80
an ounce to $471.10.

Investors, Better Hold On Tight! --- Uncertainties About Energy And Rates Suggest Markets Will Get a Lot More Volatile
Going Global
Investors, Better Hold On Tight! --- Uncertainties About Energy And Rates Suggest Markets Will Get a
Lot More Volatile
By Michael R. Sesit
790 words
13 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Paris -- IF YOU'RE PRONE to motion sickness, it's time to get out your pills because we're in for a bumpy
ride.
Money managers, economists and investment strategists warn that uncertainty over the direction of energy
prices and central-bank interest-rate polices, as well as worries about inflation and economic growth, augur
a volatile time in world financial markets. And for the superstitious, don't forget it is October: Remember 1929,
1987 and 1998?
David Bowers, chief global investment strategist at Merrill Lynch &Co., notes that the VIX, the Chicago Board
Options Exchange volatility index, is up 53% since July 20. Its German counterpart for European
stock-market volatility, the VDAX, has risen 55% since July 1.
It is rare for stocks to rally in such an environment. Indeed, following steady advances since May, most stock
markets took a dive last week. Tokyo's benchmark Nikkei Stock Average of 225 companies tumbled 3.7% in
just three days, while the Dow Jones Stoxx 50 index of major European corporations dropped 2.8% from
Tuesday to Friday. Morgan Stanley Capital International's world emerging-markets index fell 3.1% during the
past week. And on Wall Street, the already-lame Dow Jones Industrial Average shed 2.6% between Monday
and Friday.
Adding to the distress: U.S. bonds, which often move in the opposite direction to share prices, also have slid.
Since Sept. 1, the yield on 10-year U.S. Treasury bonds, which moves inversely to bond prices, has climbed
to 4.4% from 4.02%, while that on two-year Treasurys has risen to 4.23% from 3.72%.
"In an apparent wholesale downgrade of dollar-denominated assets, U.S. bond markets are taking their cue
from the drab showing of dollar stocks and also selling off," says Christopher Dunn, an independent strategist
and owner of The Good Trader Web site.
Behind the rising bond yields lie growing apprehensions that high energy prices are fueling inflationary
pressures and pushing the Federal Reserve to keep raising official interest rates, despite having done so 11
times in the past 16 months. Its sister institution, the European Central Bank, has left its key refinancing rate
unchanged at 2% for 28 consecutive months, but it is talking tough about raising rates.
While the bank appears more concerned about the potential inflation associated with high-priced energy
than the euro zone's anemic growth and high unemployment, some economists contend it might soon have to
put up or shut up when it comes to a rate increase.
David Abramson, head of European strategy at BCA Research, says that European stocks, which were doing
very well until recently, should continue to outperform bonds. But he warns that "the equity rally will become
increasingly volatile and narrowly based."
His advice: Own fewer corporate bonds than usual and use stock rallies to reduce holdings of cyclical shares
like airlines, capital goods and chemicals in order to buy defensive stocks, such as pharmaceuticals, energy
and household products. His favorite European markets are Sweden, Switzerland and Britain, all of which
have pharma-heavy stock indexes.
Tim Harris, a global strategist at J.P. Morgan Private Bank, says that "the combination of slowing global
growth and rising inflation makes risk assets, such as equities, corporate bonds and single-strategy hedge
funds, less appealing and defensive assets, such as real estate, diversified hedge-fund strategies and
specially structured equity products, more attractive."

--- Page 174 ---
Page 174 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Global stock and bond markets aren't the only venues of volatility. The price of oil fell 8.1% in the week
ending Oct. 6 before rebounding 5.1% by midday yesterday. Also yesterday, the price of gold tapped
$481.90 an ounce, its highest level in nearly 18 years.
Some investors see the "yellow metal" as a hedge against inflation, others as insurance against a
global-market meltdown and still others as protection against the retirement of Fed Chairman Alan
Greenspan, who must step down Jan. 31. In two recent reports, Morgan Stanley Chief Economist Stephen
Roach points out that the past three leadership changes at the Fed have been accompanied by market
volatility.
What is more, Mr. Roach notes that at a record 6.5% of gross domestic product, the U.S. current-account
deficit is more than four times as big as it was in 1978, 1979 or 1987, making it imperative that President
Bush appoint a tough, independent, internationally respected policy maker.
Fearing that he won't, Mr. Roach warns that "the curse of the Fed transition is about to strike again."

How to Prepare Your Portfolio to Handle Three Potential Investment Nightmares
Getting Going
How to Prepare Your Portfolio to Handle Three Potential Investment Nightmares
By Jonathan Clements
963 words
12 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BOLD CONVICTION, meet event risk.
Oil above $60 a barrel, the specter of economic weakness and fears of inflation have combined to spook
investors. Indeed, thanks to the recent stock-market slump, both the Dow Jones Industrial Average and the
Standard &Poor's 500-stock index are underwater for the year.
Yet such turmoil is par for the course. I got my first job in financial journalism 20 years ago, and I am still
waiting for things to calm down. Consider what's happened:
-- The Dow industrials plunged 22.6% on a single day in October 1987.
-- Japanese stocks peaked in 1989 -- and today are 65% lower.
-- Twice in a dozen years, stock investors were rattled when the U.S. went to war with Saddam Hussein.
-- The largest one-day point drop ever in the Dow industrials occurred when the market reopened after the
Sept. 11 terrorist attacks.
-- The S&P 500 posted 20%-plus gains in five consecutive calendar years in the late 1990s -- and then
immediately got whacked with three consecutive losing years.
Yet, despite all this turmoil, many folks continue to insist that 10% is the typical annual return for stocks. Sure
enough, the S&P 500 did indeed clock 10.9% in 2004.
But a "normal" year like 2004 is, in truth, far from normal. Over the past 20 calendar years, there have been
just three years when the S&P 500's annual return was above 6% but below 16%, according to Chicago's
Ibbotson Associates.
What about the other 17 years? Among them were nine years when the S&P 500 soared 20% or more and
four years when it lost money.
Looking good. Thanks to this year's lackluster market, stock valuations don't look so terrible. The S&P 500 is
at less than 19 times trailing 12-month reported earnings and the dividend yield now hovers around 2%.
While that may be rich by historical standards, stocks look reasonable compared with 10-year Treasury
notes, which yield less than two percentage points above the likely inflation rate.
That said, if we get a single scary headline, stocks could easily tumble 20% or more. Experts in behavioral
finance have found that investors tend to be far too confident.
Real-estate investment trusts? Gold shares? Florida condos? Energy stocks? Overconfident speculators are
convinced these highflying investments are a one-way ticket to wealth.
But a few years from now, we could be lamenting these investments the same way we now lament tech
stocks bought in the 1990s. My advice: Never forget what happened to dot-com investors -- and build your
portfolio with well-informed trepidation.
Looking down. To that end, consider three key investment pitfalls: resurgent inflation, recession and sudden
crises that shake investor confidence. In that last category, include the fallout from events like currency
devaluations, political turmoil and terrorist attacks.
How would your investments perform in each scenario? In all three situations, money-market funds, Treasury
bills and other "cash investments" would be the model of stability. But loading up on cash investments is no
solution, because you won't make money over the long run, once you figure in inflation and taxes.

--- Page 179 ---
Page 179 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Instead, to score decent gains, you've got to take more risk, and that means tapping into the stock and bond
markets. But keep your overconfidence -- and your portfolio's risk level -- in check.
Take bonds. You might spread your holdings across a mix of high-quality corporate and government bonds,
inflation-indexed Treasurys, high-yield "junk" bonds and foreign bonds, knowing that only some of these
sectors will do well at any one time.
For instance, if recession hits, junk bonds will get crushed as investors fear defaults. But your high-quality
bonds should post gains, thanks to the likely fall in interest rates. Investors will also tend to flock to
high-quality bonds, especially Treasurys, during crises of confidence.
On the other hand, conventional Treasurys would get pummeled by resurgent inflation. But in that
environment, your inflation-indexed Treasurys should hold their own. And if the renewed inflation is sparked
by an overheated economy, your junk bonds could notch impressive gains.
Foreign bonds, for their part, are a wild card, because currency moves are impossible to predict. That,
however, is part of their allure.
When everything else fails, your foreign bonds may ride to the rescue, helping to prop up your portfolio's
performance. That will be especially true if the next crisis is triggered by worries over U.S. debt levels.
You can apply the same analysis to various classes of stocks, except the potential gains and losses are far
larger. While an inflationary spike might dent stocks initially, shares should fare well longer term, as corporate
earnings climb with inflation.
Recession would also cause short-term trouble for stocks. Meanwhile, it's hard to know how shares will react
to the next crisis. Given all that, you should spread your bets widely, always owning at least some bonds and
allocating maybe 25% of your stock portfolio to foreign shares, 5% to real-estate investment trusts and 5% to
gold stocks, commodities and other natural-resource plays.
REITs and natural resources should perform well if inflation picks up, and foreign stocks could save your
portfolio if the dollar nose-dives. But who really knows? Investing is racked with uncertainty -- and broad
diversification is your best defense.
---
Triple Threat
Here are three possible pitfalls facing investors -- and the investments
that are likely to flourish in each scenario.
-- Recessions: Bonds -- especially long-term government, municipal and
high-quality corporate bonds.
--
Inflation: Gold stocks, commodities, real estate, inflation-indexed
bonds.
-- Crises of confidence: Treasury bonds, gold stocks.

Page 180 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
`I Read the News Today, Oh Boy'
Global View
`I Read the News Today, Oh Boy'
By George Melloan
1,043 words
11 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In "A Day in the Life," John Lennon captured the sense of resignation generated by the daily diet of doom and
gloom fed to us by broadcasters and newspapers. He and his Beatles collaborator Paul McCartney wrote it
all off with comic inanities: "Now they know how many holes it takes to fill the Albert Hall."
Yet it's hard to shrug off earthquakes, fires, floods, bombings, warfare and all the other images that flood in
on us thanks to the miracle of modern communications. We not only hear of the event but the often-terrible
stories the survivors have to tell, the accounts of officials, and the instant commentary on what it all means.
When an event can be predicted, weather forecasters and emergency-preparedness officials predict the
worst to avoid accusations later of criminal complacency.
If there is any comfort in all this, it sometimes can be found by sharply discounting apocalyptic theories,
including breathless predictions of economic collapse, massive social disorder, energy shortages or
pandemics. More often than not, over-the-top forecasts are the product of the innate need in all of us to get
attention, a need that can now be gratified through access to microphones and TV cameras that transmit
words and images instantly to a world-wide audience.
Especially in need of attention are those of us in the news media. Our jobs and the fate of our enterprises
depend on it -- hence, the occasional temptation to exaggerate. For government officials, no problem means
no appropriation from the legislature -- so make it as big as you can. All those non-governmental
organizations (NGOs) need to attract donations.
In all cases, there's a penalty for being wrong -- the loss of reputation -- but tomorrow's massive news budget
will blot from the collective consciousness most of the nonsense uttered today. If you write a best-selling book
that predicts hard times in, say, five years, you will have banked your royalties well before anyone knows if
you were right or wrong.
Looking back into both the recent and distant past offers support for this thesis. Hurricane Katrina was of
course a big disaster, flooding large numbers of Louisianans and Mississippians out of their homes and
businesses, causing an estimated $35 billion in insured property losses, severe damage to the Gulf-centered
oil and gas industry and nearly 1,000 deaths. Yet the U.S. economy only hiccupped, as was made plain by
last Friday's report of only a slight decline in employment in September.
A lot of "news" that went out over the airwaves didn't happen, as Mark Steyn comments in the October 3 New
York Sun. There was no rampage of rape and murder in the Superdome, no 10,000 dead. One man was
arrested for firing at a helicopter but he may have been signalling for a rescue. Mr. Steyn, a bit of a partisan
himself, saw in all this misreporting a concerted campaign to discredit George W. Bush. Who knows? The
idea that political zealotry sometimes infects the minds of newshounds isn't totally implausible.
But let's go back nearly 20 years, to Chernobyl, a disaster from which the nuclear-power industry has not yet
recovered. When the reactor in Ukraine exploded in April 1986 during a clumsy test procedure, the scare
stories had Laplanders losing their reindeer herds, distant Germans dying of radiation sickness and forecasts
of as many as 800,000 additional cancers in humans over the next 70 years.
A new and exhaustive study by United Nations and governmental agencies assesses the damage 20 years
on. Only 56 people have died from causes related to Chernobyl radiation, 47 of them plant personnel killed by
the blast or in fighting the fire that resulted. Some 4,000 children developed thyroid cancer but nearly all were
cured and only nine died. The study projects 4,000 cancers over time, mostly among workers directly
exposed to blast radiation, a far cry from the 1986 alarums.
The restricted area around Chernobyl has become a veritable "game park," says one U.N. observer. Because
of hunting restrictions, deer, wild boar, bison and other creatures roam freely and in good health.

--- Page 181 ---
Page 181 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Environmental radicals who want to return forests to their natural state could do worse than using a nuclear
blast to drive out all the humans -- but don't get any ideas, please. The U.N. estimates that 70% of the area
could be safely returned to productive use.
Those dire predictions of 1986 can be excused partly by ignorance. Nuclear radiation had been studied
extensively by then, using the only other examples of mass exposure, at Hiroshima and Nagasaki. But those
were results of atom bombs, whereas the main damage from Chernobyl was the release of radioactive
particles. It can be suspected that some of the reporting was tainted with hysteria fanned by a powerful and
ubiquitous environmentalist lobby that had targeted nuclear power for destruction.
So what's the latest big scare story? It would seem to be avian flu. President Bush himself is looking into
whether Americans are prepared for a threatened "pandemic." Federal agencies, naturally, are saying they
need more budget. But keep in mind that while human flu can be highly contagious and dangerous, the
transmission of animal diseases to humans is rare, according to some of the more trustworthy
epidemiologists.
Maybe we should take a hint from Peter Cameron, a laid-back columnist on Australia's tranquil Gold Coast:
"Australians ignore the threat of suicide bombers in Bali but fear everything else from the old wives' cupboard.
Up to 50 million people are predicted to perish from avian influenza if there is a pandemic, yet less than 800
died from SARS.
"All this scaremongering goes on when cardiovascular disease accounts for about 40% of Australian deaths
each year. CVD, pardon the pun, leaves AIDS, cancer and strokes for dead when it comes to killing capacity.
"What about malaria and tuberculosis? Millions die from these diseases in the Third World. The response of
Western nations is to turn a blind eye and spend millions and millions preparing for avian influenza."
Well said, mate. And I hope you're right.

Would You Like To Pay by Check, Cash -- or Gold? --- James Turk's Quixotic Quest: An Online Payment System Based Entirely on Bullion
Would You Like To Pay by Check, Cash -- or Gold? --- James Turk's Quixotic Quest: An Online
Payment System Based Entirely on Bullion
By Craig Karmin
1,166 words
8 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
JAMES TURK thinks he has a solution to worries about a weaker dollar: Stop using cash to pay for things --
instead, pay with gold.
A handful of companies are pitching services that let people make payments to one another denominated in
gold, much as they might wire cash through a bank account. Amid soaring bullion prices and simmering
concerns about the health of the U.S. economy, they are finding a small but promising market.
One of the main providers, GoldMoney.com, based in the British Channel Islands and founded by Mr. Turk,
currently has 23,000 users, he says, more than double the number a year ago. His company followed on the
heels of e-gold.com, a West Indies-based company started by a Florida oncologist that has offered electronic
gold payments since 1996. A handful of other competitors, with names such as e-bullion.com, also have
sprung up.
"Gold's historic role has always been as the world's currency," says Mr. Turk, a 58-year-old former banker
and self-described "gold bug." That is a term more often applied to fringe characters who own gold to protect
against market crashes or even Armageddon.
"Some gold bugs are very fervent," Mr. Turk says.
Still, he shares their belief that governments ultimately give in to temptation by printing too much money and
debasing their currencies. He argues that current U.S. economic fundamentals -- rising government spending
paired with an increasing trade deficit -- will inevitably cause the dollar to weaken dramatically.
There are mainstream economists who share some of his concerns, even if they disagree with his proposed
solution. And gold could prove to be a wise investment, because it tends to gain value at times of uncertainty.
But it is hardly a one-way bet. For the past 25 years, holding gold actually has been a good way to reduce
wealth. While bullion prices recently hit $472.30 an ounce, their best level since 1988, that is down from $834
an ounce in 1980.
GoldMoney's users tend to be small-business owners who make regular purchases overseas and worry
about currency fluctuations. Jeff Wright, a director for software-development company TimeWarp in Colorado
Springs, Colo., has been using GoldMoney for more than three years to buy software from vendors in Europe
and Australia. One benefit, he says, is that everyone avoids currency-conversion charges, which can be as
much as thousands of dollars on large transactions.
Of course, now he has to worry about volatility in gold prices. "I usually check it twice a week to see how
things are going," he says.
Services like these aren't for everybody. For one thing, Mr. Wright points out that the first thing he had to do
was persuade his suppliers to accept payments in gold and then set up online accounts with GoldMoney.
And the gold industry has proven to be rife with scams and swindlers. During the early 1980s, for instance,
customers of the International Gold Bullion Exchange in Fort Lauderdale, Fla., once the largest gold-bullion
dealer in the U.S., were shocked to learn that the gold bars stored in the company's vault were made of
wood. Thousands of customers lost tens of millions of dollars.
Mr. Turk says he often fields questions about authenticity, and points out that he lists the vault company
where the gold is stored on the company's Web site, as well as other documents attesting to the gold's
validity. His customers' holdings are valued at $62 million, Mr. Turk says. Account holders' money is held in
gold that is stored as 400-ounce bars in a vault just outside London.

--- Page 187 ---
Page 187 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In effect, each time a payment is made, one account holder is passing to another a claim on the stack of gold
in the London vault. He says a transaction costs only about $1.50, compared with $20 or more for a bank
wire.
Mr. Turk's own thoughts about the nature of money go back to his childhood in Ohio, where his Austrian
father emigrated after World War I. After the war, Austria suffered extreme hyperinflation that made its
currency next to worthless.
Then, early in his banking career, while with Chase Manhattan in Asia, he was present in Bangkok in 1974 for
the collapse of Herstatt Bank due to unauthorized foreign-exchange dealings. It marked the biggest bank
failure in the history of what was then West Germany, and caused turbulence in financial markets around the
globe.
"My family's experience and Herstatt's collapse made me realize that national currencies are much more
inherently unstable than tangible assets," he says. That led him to start thinking about how to circulate gold
as a currency, a practice that started falling out of favor back in the 17th century, when the Bank of England
issued the first widely circulated paper money as a stand-in for gold and silver.
After a stint in the United Arab Emirates, where he managed the precious-metals portfolio for the Abu Dhabi
Investment Authority, Mr. Turk settled in London, where since 1987 he has published the Freemarket Gold
&Money Report.
His newsletter quickly became essential reading for gold bugs, a group he has an affinity for despite its
quirks. In one instance, in the late 1990s a gold-bug organization called the Gold Anti-Trust Action
Committee demanded a congressional investigation into its claim that, in effect, Federal Reserve Chairman
Alan Greenspan was conspiring to fix gold prices with the Bank of England and the government of Kuwait.
"The framers of the Constitution were gold bugs," Mr. Turk says. "That's why they insisted on a sound money
policy of gold and silver when writing the Constitution."
In 1998, as e-commerce was taking off, Mr. Turk launched GoldMoney, seeing the Internet as a tool enabling
gold to again be used as currency. Last year the company reported revenue of $37 million, Mr. Turk says,
and he expects twice that much this year. The company has attracted outside investment from DRD Gold, a
South African mining company, and IAMGold Corp. in Toronto, which together hold a 21% stake in
GoldMoney.
Mr. Turk's case for bullion is rooted in history. He argues that all governments -- from ancient Rome to King
Louis XV's France to 1990s Argentina -- eventually succumb to excessive spending. Rather than raise taxes,
officials resort to printing more money, sparking inflation and financial collapse.
Because paper currencies are no longer backed by gold or another tangible asset, Mr. Turk likes to point out,
they represent nothing more than a government's promise to honor them. But when currencies fall, he says,
gold remains a valuable commodity.
"Unlike the dollar," he says, "gold is not dependent on the U.S. government's promise to honor it."

Page 189 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

MarketWatch Weekend Investor / Best-Performing Sectors
By Tomi Kilgore
542 words
8 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal

B4

Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS WHO FOLLOW economic trends in search of opportunity should not be surprised, given rising
oil prices and mounting fears of inflation, that energy and raw-materials sectors were the winners in the third
quarter.
But who would have guessed that consumer electronics would vault to the No. 2 spot among the quarter's
best performers?
The continued frenzy over Apple Computer Inc.'s iPod digital-music player has spilled over into electronic
gadgets of all sorts, fueling gains in the Dow Jones U.S. Consumer Electronics Index, which shot up 33% in

The biggest winners included entertainment-systems company Harman International Industries Inc., which

concert halls.
Other gainers include CD and DVD recordable publishing systems maker Rimage Corp., Panasonic parent
Matsushita Electric Industrial Co. and mobile-navigation-device maker Cobra Electronics Corp.
And just identifying the energy sector as a place to invest might not have tipped you to the best place to have
your money in the third quarter: coal, as surging oil and gas prices sent investors searching for a cheaper
alternative. Peabody Energy Corp., Foundation Coal Holdings Inc. and James River Coal Co. helped lift the
Dow Jones U.S. Coal Index to a 37% gain over the past three months.
Investors looking for sectors with upside potential in the coming months might do well to keep an eye on raw
materials, given that Fed officials continue to express inflation concerns. Top-performing
raw-materials-producing sectors in the third quarter included the Dow Jones U.S. Nonferrous Metals Index
and the Dow Jones U.S. Mining Index, which each gained 28%, and the Dow Jones U.S. Steel Index, which
advanced 22%.
---

THE JOB MARKET SHOWED surprising strength last month despite Hurricane Katrina's impact.
Unemployment rose to 5.1% but payrolls fell far less than expected. The data suggest that the U.S. economy
is likely to recover well from Katrina, keeping the Fed focused on containing inflation by lifting rates.
The Dow industrials edged up 5.21 points to 10292.31, helped by the jobs report, but finished the week down
2.6%. Oil rose to $61.84.
---
Delphi may file for Chapter 11 as soon as today, leaving workers to face job cuts, slashed medical benefits
and uncertain retirements.

A Merck witness's testimony was struck by the judge in the second Vioxx trial, ruling that the drug maker
misled the court.

plan for an IPO.

gambling businesses.

for some $750 million.

News Corp. shareholders sued to block a poison-pill extension, which could undermine Murdoch's ability to

RIM suffered a setback in its battle with NTP, reopening the way for the patent-holding firm to seek to block

chairman's leadership.

STOCKS TUMBLED after Fed officials suggested the central bank is worried about inflation and is likely to
keep lifting rates. The Dow industrials slid 123.75 points to 10317.36, their lowest close since July 7; the S&P
500 and Nasdaq also finished at three-month lows. The declines came despite a $1.11 pullback in oil to
$62.79. Treasury prices rose.
---
Katrina evacuees face possible financial disaster as money to pay workers dries up, banks get tough on
borrowers and snafus slow the flow of cash and loans.
Katrina-related losses for insurers will range from $40 billion to $55 billion, according to an estimate by
Towers Perrin.
---
The hurricanes have hurt manufacturers who depend on materials derived from oil and natural gas, such as
foam and resin.
---
FedEx plans to boost air-shipment rates by 5.5%, the largest increase in at least nine years, amid strong
demand for deliveries.
---
Service-sector growth slowed considerably in September amid worries about higher costs, according to the
monthly ISM survey.
---
GM is selling its 20% stake in Fuji, driving the Subaru maker into a relationship with Toyota.
Toyota is boosting its stake to 60% in Panasonic EV, a maker of batteries for hybrid engines.
---
The ECB is likely to intensify its inflation warnings, increasing the chance of a future rate rise despite slow
growth in Europe.
---
Italy's government approved a decree providing $143 million in benefits to the airline industry in an effort to
help Alitalia.
---
MCI shareholders vote today on Verizon's $8.4 billion takeover. The deal is expected to win approval by a
large margin.
---
Holders of a catastrophe bond issued by Swiss Re could lose all of their investment as the result of
Katrina-related claims.

Indonesia sold $1.5 billion in bonds, as demand for emerging-market debt eclipsed concerns about oil prices
and terrorism.

KPMG's sale of tax shelters to City National has raised conflict concerns, since KPMG is also the bank's
outside auditor.
---
Motorola will cut about 1,900 jobs, or 3% of its staff. Separately, sales of its Rokr music phone have been
slower than expected.
---
Apple next week is expected to launch an iPod that can play video and begin selling music videos through its
iTunes store.
---
EA agreed to settle a suit by ex-employees who say the videogame firm owes them overtime pay.

Yum's net rose 16%, bolstered by growth in China and strength in its KFC and Taco Bell brands.

A GRAPHIC on mutual-fund performance in the third quarter of 2005 on page R4 of yesterday's Mutual
Funds Quarterly Review showed the value of $10,000 invested at the end of the second quarter of 2005 in
the average fund of several different classifications, including emerging-markets stock funds. In some
editions, the chart incorrectly said that the $10,000 investment was made at the end of the third quarter of
2005 and that the emerging-markets funds were bond funds.
---
REP. TOM DELAY was admonished by the House Ethics Committee for trying to obtain a vote on the
Medicare bill by promising then-Rep. Nick Smith of Michigan that he would provide campaign support to his
son. Saturday's Hot Topic incorrectly said Mr. DeLay obtained the vote. Rep. Smith voted against the bill.
(See: "REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial) -- Hot Topic: Republicans DeLayed" -- WSJ Oct. 1, 2005)

STOCKS FELL on news of a big jump in factory orders, which some investors took as a sign of potential
inflation, and hints of further rate increases by the Dallas Fed president. Profit warnings also hurt share
prices. The Dow industrials closed down 94.37 points at 10441.11. Crude oil futures fell $1.57 to $63.90 on
signs of slowing demand and greater supply.
Bush said that his choice for a new Federal Reserve chairman to succeed Alan Greenspan must be
independent of politics.
---
A private-equity group made a bid to buy TDC, the second offer the Danish telecom company has received in
recent months.
---
A suit against Delphi alleges the auto supplier's senior executives encouraged inventory deals that artificially
boosted profits.
---
U.S. firms plan to repatriate about $206 billion under a tax break, but most of the funds aren't likely to go to
direct job creation.
---
China Construction Bank today begins what could be China's largest IPO, as the lender seeks to raise as
much as $7.7 billion.
---
BP expects a profit hit of over $700 million from Hurricanes Katrina and Rita and sees hydrocarbon output
trailing forecasts.
---
Lexmark halved its earnings estimate for the third quarter and predicted a slow fourth period, pushing its
stock down 29%.

Sun and Google announced a software alliance that could open a new front in Google's heated competition
with Microsoft.
---
The Airbus A380 may spark a rift between the U.S. and Europe over potential safety hazards created by the
jet's wake.
---
Continental forecast a modest third-quarter profit as it and some other airlines posted stronger September
passenger traffic.

Two Samsung executives were convicted of selling corporate bonds to members of the firm's founding family

Italian dairy firm Granarolo confirmed that it was considering a bid for Parmalat, kicking off a potential

WPP's Mindshare will lose Gillette's media buying and planning duties because it does work for Unilever, a

Blue Chips Fall On Inflation Sign To Start Quarter
Monday's Markets
Blue Chips Fall On Inflation Sign To Start Quarter
By E.S. Browning
503 words
4 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER BRIEFLY trying to bid stocks higher to start the new quarter, investors got a whiff of inflation, and
sent blue-chip shares back down.
Although technology stocks and small stocks finished the day ahead, and the Dow Jones Utility Average hit
another record, big-stock indexes declined. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 33.22 points, or 0.31%, to
10535.48, ending a streak of five consecutive gains. It is down 2.3% for 2005.
The culprit was a seemingly positive report on robust September manufacturing activity. Normally it would
have sent stocks higher. But the report also said that prices paid by manufacturers had jumped. That
reinforced fears that hurricanes Katrina and Rita and government relief spending would lift prices, forcing the
Federal Reserve to keep raising short-term interest rates to cool the economy.
Rising rates hamper both corporate profits and consumer spending, and also make existing bonds look less
attractive. Bond prices sagged on the report, which hit in midmorning, pushing bond yields higher. Stocks
followed bonds down.
"I was hoping to get a big rally with people putting money to work for the fourth quarter," said Todd Leone,
head of listed trading at brokerage firm SG Cowen. "But once that number came out, it took a little bit of a toll
on the market. This is just another reason why the Fed might continue to raise rates."
Futures on unleaded gasoline, crude oil and gold all fell on the day, which ordinarily would have helped major
stocks. But lately, inflation has been rising on the list of investor worries, and yesterday it worried investors.
Natural gas, however, rose for the fifth session in the past six, and has doubled in price in less than a year
amid worries about heavy demand and short supplies. Rising natural-gas prices helped fuel the utility-stock
rally, as investors bet that utilities would be expanding and charging higher rates for electricity and gas.
The Dow utilities rose 1.21%, or 5.25 points, to 437.63, up 31% this year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.17%, or 3.74 points, to 2155.43, on hopes that a robust economy would
fuel corporate and consumer demand for tech goods. It still is down 0.9% in 2005.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock Index fell 0.17%, or 2.11 points, to 1226.70, up 1.2% this year.
The prospect of higher interest rates pushed up the dollar.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed, and bonds fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 15/32, or $4.69 for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield up to 4.389%. The 30-year bond was down a full point to yield 4.628%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 114.14 yen, up from 113.52, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1916 from $1.2021.

Business and Finance
Business and Finance
What's News
Business and Finance
516 words
4 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SUV SALES PLUNGED at GM and Ford during September, and resale values also dropped, in the strongest
evidence so far that consumers are seeking smaller, more fuel-efficient vehicles since gas prices jumped to
around $3 a gallon after hurricanes Katrina and Rita.
---
Manufacturing turned up in September despite Katrina and a spike in energy prices, but raw-materials prices
also rose, in a sign of incipient inflation.
---
Oil exporters are investing more of their record windfall from surging fuel prices into the U.S. and Europe than
in past booms.
Russian oil output is showing signs of recovery after nearly a year and may re-emerge as a critical global
source of new crude.
---
Microsoft ended licensing talks with four global music giants, raising queries about the software firm's
music-service plans.
---
Steel-industry leaders worry that China's growing steel overcapacity may lower prices for the metal despite
growing global demand for their products.
---
U.S. stocks retreated 33.22 points to 10535.48 as the September rise in producers' prices fanned inflation
fears. Oil prices declined 77 cents to $65.47. The euro fell to $1.1916.
---
Reliant plans to raise Houston-area electricity prices by 14% in the short term, and possibly by an additional
10% in January.
---
Brandywine's plan to acquire Prentiss Properties in a $1.92 billion deal will form one of the biggest office
REITs in the country.
---
Telefonica's reported interest in KPN would be in line with a shift in the Spanish telecom's focus from Latin
America to Europe.
---
Endesa plans to distribute $8.4 billion in dividends within five years, as it defends itself against Gas Natural's
hostile bid.
---

R.H. Donnelley agreed to buy Dex Media for $4.2 billion to form the third-largest phone-directory firm in the
U.S. by revenue.
---
The SEC is drawing criticism over its decision to allow the smallest public companies more time to comply
with Sarbanes-Oxley "internal control" rules.
---
Goldman Sachs will invest $500 million to $550 million via its private-equity arm for a 9.5% stake in Hana
Financial Group.
---
British Airways' new CEO, Willie Walsh, said he aims to focus on developing its business even as he
continues cost cutting.
---
Boots and Alliance UniChem confirmed they will combine into a health-and-beauty retailer with annual sales
of over $23 billion.
---
MCI will pay $331 million to settle back-tax disputes with 16 states and the District of Columbia.

Photronics, Cleco, OmniVision Gain; Plantronics Falls
Small-Stock Focus
Photronics, Cleco, OmniVision Gain; Plantronics Falls
By Anjali Cordeiro Dow Jones Newswires
513 words
4 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Small stocks rose, adding to Friday's gains and doing better than the broad market, with the semiconductor
and electricity sectors performing well.
A report by the Semiconductor Industry Association, which said that world-wide sales of chips rose in August
and that the outlook for sales remains strong, pushed semiconductor stocks higher. OmniVision Technologies
gained 79 cents, or 6.3%, to $13.41; Photronics added 35 cents, or 1.8%, to 19.75; and Triquint
Semiconductor rose 12 cents, or 3.4%, to 3.64.
The electricity sector saw gains after large-cap NRG Energy said it will buy privately held power generator
Texas Genco. Cleco (NYSE) added 47 cents, or 2%, to 24.05; Idacorp (NYSE) gained 46 cents, or 1.5%, to
30.59; and Allete (NYSE) rose 69 cents, or 1.5%, to 46.50.
The market was focused on "the economic data and trying to figure out what the Federal Reserve's next
move on interest rates is going to be," said Mike Viracola, managing director and head of equity trading at
Adams Harkness in Boston.
Small caps were able to finish the day in positive territory despite the mixed economic news. The Russell
2000 index added 2.75, or 0.41%, to 670.55. The S&P SmallCap 600 Index gained 1.86, or 0.53%, to 352.06.
The Nasdaq Composite Index of large- and small-cap stocks added 3.74, or 0.17%, to 2155.43.
A number of precious-metal stocks were down after gold and silver futures pulled back, helped by the
combination of a strong dollar and weak crude prices. Desert Sun Mining (Amex) fell three cents, or 1.7%, to
1.77 and Cambior (Amex) dropped six cents, or 2.8%, to 2.07.
Lexar Media added 1.15, or 18%, to 7.53. J.P. Morgan upgraded the stock to overweight from neutral, citing
the Fremont, Calif., digital-media manufacturer's recent deal with Sony to expand their license agreement.
School Specialty fell 8.42, or 17%, to 40.36. The Greenville, Wis., provider of supplemental-learning products
said its purchase by an affiliate of Bain Capital wouldn't close as expected this week because three banks
that had agreed to provide $350 million in financing backed out.
ShopKo Stores (NYSE) gained 76 cents, or 3%, to 26.28. The Green Bay, Wis., discount-retail chain received
an unsolicited offer from a group of investors to be acquired for $26.50 a share, just days after a Minneapolis
private-equity firm sweetened its bid for the second time.
Cavalry Bancorp gained 3.59, or 18%, to 23.19. Pinnacle Financial Partners agreed to buy the Murfreesboro,
Tenn., financial institution in a transaction Pinnacle valued at about $175.5 million in stock. Pinnacle fell 49
cents, or 2%, to 24.69.
Plantronics (NYSE) fell 2.60, or 8.4%, to 28.21. The Santa Cruz, Calif., communications headset company
said its second-quarter revenue will be lower than projected.

Stock Market Quarterly Review: Crude Oil Paces Commodities Rally --- Led by Highflying Energy Sector, Indexes Are Near Highs for Year; Signs...
Stock Market Quarterly Review: Crude Oil Paces Commodities Rally --- Led by Highflying Energy
Sector, Indexes Are Near Highs for Year; Signs of Inflation Spark Concern
By Peter A. McKay
990 words
3 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Soaring energy prices have grabbed most of the attention since the hurricanes that smashed into the Gulf
Coast, but the rest of the commodity sector also is booming, with no end in sight in the fourth quarter,
economists say.
Both the broad-based Dow Jones-AIG Index of 20 commodities and the Reuters/Jefferies CRB Index,
another broad-based commodity indicator, are near their highs for the year. Zinc is near an eight-year high at
around $1,420 a metric ton, copper is near a record, at $1.7275 a pound, and lumber is up almost 10% since
Hurricane Katrina struck in late August -- each boosted in part by its necessity in the vast rebuilding effort
along the Gulf Coast. A metric ton is equal to 2,204.62 pounds.
Led by the energy sector, the Dow Jones-AIG Index ended the third quarter up 16.6%, or 25.37 points, at
178.25, while the CRB index rose 8.7%, or 26.66 points, to 332.97.
Although the price of crude oil is back near pre-Hurricane Katrina levels, at $66.24 a barrel, natural gas has
posted the biggest gains, up more than 42% since the storm hit in late August and nearly double in price for
the quarter, to $13.921 a million British thermal units. Inventories of natural gas and heating oil are low
heading into winter -- a situation expected to produce massive heating bills for consumers.
Gold futures are up 6.8%, or $30.20, since the first storm struck, and are up 8.1% for the quarter, near an
18-year high at $472.30 a troy ounce in New York trading -- a sign of rising inflation fears.
Investors' attention seems to have quickly shifted to possible inflation -- and away from a possible short-term
slowdown in economic growth -- following Hurricane Katrina. Initially, traders worried that consumers would
be spooked, and that skyrocketing gasoline prices would cut into consumers' purchases of other goods. Wall
Street professionals even pondered whether the Federal Reserve might end its series of interest-rate
increases to prod Americans to keep spending.
That changed quickly. The $200 billion-plus President Bush has promised in federal relief was viewed by
investors as economic stimulus. Bottlenecks at ports proved to be a manageable hindrance, and Hurricane
Rita turned out to be a weaker follow-up storm. The Fed raised rates again by a quarter-point in September.
Commodity prices -- often a barometer of inflationary pressure -- took off again.
The hurricanes hit as the bull market for raw materials, which had run for more than three years, was
considered to be past its prime by many analysts. Now, many economists say they don't expect a significant
pullback in commodity demand and prices until sometime next year, perhaps by June.
"We're now in the phase of the economic cycle when you should start to pay attention to these successive
rounds of price increases," says economist Ethan Harris of Lehman Brothers. "Usually, people just shrug off
commodity-price inflation until they see" a long-term rally.
One notable exception to recent commodity gains has been grain-futures prices, which fell when investors
feared U.S. exports wouldn't be able to get out of the Port of New Orleans. Because such contracts
guarantee delivery of grain stored in U.S. warehouses, their prices tend to go down if traders are worried that
stockpiles are stranded, unable to get to markets overseas that are supposed to be their final destination.
Those fears soon eased, but then bumper harvests sparked a fresh wave of selling.
On the Chicago Board of Trade, contracts on corn soybeans and corn are off more than 4% since Hurricane
Katrina and are off more than 11% for the quarter. Wheat futures bucked the trend, up 4.4% on the quarter
and 5.7% post-Hurricane Katrina, at $3.4625 a bushel on the CBOT.
The recent strength in most of the commodity sector is unusual because it has come in the absence of a
weakening dollar, says Richard W. Asplund, chief economist for the Commodity Research Bureau.

--- Page 215 ---
Page 215 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the first few years of commodities' latest run-up, many analysts cited a weaker dollar as a catalyst. Most
commodities are traded globally in dollar terms. When the dollar declines in value, it takes more dollars to buy
the same amount of resources, so prices tend to increase, all other factors being equal.
Mr. Asplund and other experts believe this year's gains have been demand driven, because the Federal
Reserve's series of rate increases have, in effect, taken dollars out of circulation, supported the currency's
value, and eliminated it as a concern for commodity traders.
The Fed is widely expected to stop raising interest rates early next year. If that happens, commodities could
take off again, in a surge that could finally push prices high enough to prompt users to cut back, Mr. Asplund
says, thus causing prices to decline.
"Until that point, you could just see commodity prices chopping around in a tight range the rest of the year,"
he says.
Economist James Glassman of J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. expects to see an economic "soft spot" in the first
half of 2006, after the effect of big heating bills begins to hit consumers, leading them to cut back other
purchases.
Unlike some of his peers, though, Mr. Glassman isn't too worried about inflation in the latest commodity rally.
"The thing that keeps the higher prices from being inflationary is that businesses are saving money on labor,
because of low-cost competition from China and other places," he says. "If businesses spend more on
commodities, but less on labor, they keep their overall spending in check."

Fed Revives Yield-Curve Flattening
Credit Markets
Fed Revives Yield-Curve Flattening
By Shayna Stoyko Dow Jones Newswires
776 words
1 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
For a while, it looked like it might have been curtains for the one seemingly sure bet left in the Treasury
market: a flatter yield curve.
In the aftermath of one of the worst natural disasters in U.S. history, investors were suddenly forced after
Hurricane Katrina slammed into the Gulf Coast to question whether the Federal Reserve would continue to
raise rates, which earlier in Sept, caused yields on the short-dated maturities most sensitive to Fed policy to
fall much faster than their long-dated peers. That caused the benchmark yield curve -- which measures the
gap between the two-and 10-year notes -- to widen, or steepen, stopping a long flattening trend in its tracks.
Yields move inversely to prices.
But now that Fed officials are ringing the rate-rise bell loud and clear again, wagers are back on that the gap
between yields of two- and 10-year notes will narrow, resulting in a flatter curve.
Betting on a flatter yield curve has been a profitable strategy for investors, who anticipate long-dated bonds
garnering support from rate rises warding off inflation. At the same time, the slim gap between short- and
long-dated yields pressures banks and financial institutions, because it raises their short-term cost of
borrowing while lowering the longer-term rates they can charge on loans to customers.
The re-emergence of investor confidence about the direction of the Fed's near-term moves sent the yield on
the two-year note to an intraday high Friday of 4.19% -- its highest level in more than four years and
significantly above 4.02% seen at the start of the week.
Late Friday, the price of the 10-year Treasury note stood at 99 11/32, down 10/32, to yield 4.33%. The
30-year bond was off 12/32 to 112 3/32, yielding 4.57%.
The five-year note was off 7/32 to 98 19/32, yielding 4.19%, while the two-year issue was down 3/32 to 99
21/32 to yield 4.18%.
As the two-year's yield heads up toward that of the 10-year, the difference between these key yields plumbed
a new post-Katrina intraday low of 0.14 percentage point -- only 0.03 point above the fresh 4 1/2-year low
seen just before Katrina wreaked her havoc on the Gulf Coast. Many analysts say the benchmark yield curve
is likely to test its pre-Katrina lows, but some see the flattening limited beyond that point.
While economic data continue to point to a healthy economy likely to hold up amid the negative effects of the
hurricanes, inflation expectations -- as measured by gold prices and break-even levels on inflation-linked
Treasurys -- have begun to increase, said John Canavan, bond-market strategist at Stone &McCarthy
Research Associates in Princeton, N.J. Even though inflation statistics have remained quite tame, "they're
heading in the wrong direction," Mr. Canavan said. That has potential to hit the long-dated bonds most
sensitive to price pressures.
Economic data released Friday spooked inflation-wary investors, putting some pressure on the longer end of
the yield curve and causing it to move off its intraday low.
A key inflation yardstick contained in the Commerce Department's personal-income and spending report
showed inflation crept right to the top of the Fed's comfort zone. David Ader, head government-bond
strategist at RBS Greenwich Capital in Greenwich, Conn., called that "scary," and said it "adds fuel to the
Fed's argument to continue hiking."
The Fed has raised its target federal-funds rate 11 straight times since June 2004, with its most-recent move
coming on Sept. 20. Its target short-term rate stands at 3.75%.

--- Page 217 ---
Page 217 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The prices-paid component of the Chicago Purchasing Management Association's index surged to 76.3 in
September from 62.9 in August. Economists at Bear Stearns said they "expect the national ISM
manufacturing reading on Monday will tell a similar inflation story," as the Chicago data can serve as a proxy
for the national report.
As two- and 10-year note yields converge to levels last seen before Katrina, futures markets have returned to
levels last seen before the storm.
The November federal-funds futures contract is almost fully priced for a 0.25-point rate increase from the Fed
at its Nov. 1 meeting, which would take the target funds rate to 4%. The December federal-funds contract
shows the market mostly pricing in a second increase to 4.25% on Dec. 13.

New Tools for the Nest Egg --- Products Tell Baby Boomers How to Spend in Autumn Years; Weapons-Grade Budgeting
New Tools for the Nest Egg --- Products Tell Baby Boomers How to Spend in Autumn Years;
Weapons-Grade Budgeting
By Tom Lauricella
1,613 words
1 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE GOOD NEWS is you'll probably live longer than you think. The bad news is you may last longer than
your savings.
For decades, financial-services companies have pushed people to save more for retirement, but they offered
little advice on how to track and spend the money once you're in your autumn years. Now the race is on
among some big Wall Street firms to fill that void -- a bid for supremacy as the baby-boomer generation
heads for retirement.
Their primary target: Individuals who will be counting on income from multiple sources, which can include
mutual funds, 401(k) plans, Social Security checks, insurance policies and real-estate investments. The
stakes are particularly high since fewer retirees have the guaranteed lifetime income of pension plans and
corporations are rolling back retiree-health benefits. In addition, retirees are living longer on average after
retirement than people who left the work force as recently as a decade ago.
The result is a range of new tools and accounts designed to help retirees manage their finances after the
paychecks stop.
Some of these planning aids are also useful for investors approaching retirement who want to see if they're
really on track. In fact, people who have left their retirement-savings strategy on autopilot for the past few
years may find that assumptions have changed significantly about key calculations, such as how much
money can be safely withdrawn annually in retirement.
In coming weeks, Merrill Lynch &Co. plans a gradual rollout of a service that consolidates finances and also
helps retirees plan for expenses ranging from quarterly tax payments to assisting with a grandchild's
education costs. The account is available at no additional cost for clients with more than $20,000 in a Merrill
account.
Vanguard Group Inc. this month started offering similar retirement-planning tools on its Web site,
vanguard.com, including worksheets and calculators that tackle complex decisions such as when to start
withdrawing money from tax-sheltered accounts and other holdings. The tool is available to non-Vanguard
customers at no cost. (See related article on Page C4.)
OppenheimerFunds Inc. in August started offering a free budgeting tool through financial planners that sell
the company's funds. It uses powerful statistical-analysis techniques -- once used to help develop nuclear
weapons -- that gauge the odds an investor will actually end up having enough money to last the rest of his or
her life. One limitation: The portfolio recommendations consider only Oppenheimer funds.
These companies join mutual-fund giant Fidelity Investments, which last year started offering its own new
financial-planning product for retirees, called the Income Management Account. These accounts pretty much
work like any other brokerage account: At the heart, they're simply repositories for investments, and can
accept deposits from any number of outside sources, whether Social Security checks or income from a rental
property.
Where they differ, however, is that the new accounts are also tied directly to retirement incomes and investing
plans.
For instance, investors using the Fidelity account first go through the process of analyzing their spending
needs. That enables the account to include bells and whistles such as real-time email warnings if there are
signs an account-holder may be going off-track from their spending plan.
The Fidelity account can also be set up to automatically keep track of investments held outside of Fidelity, a
feature the Merrill account doesn't currently offer. Merrill also doesn't offer email alerts -- instead, the

--- Page 219 ---
Page 219 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
account's investment adviser is the one who lets investors know if they're heading for trouble. The Fidelity
Income Management Account costs $50 a year, though that fee can be waived if investors meet certain
requirements such as receiving electronic statements or maintaining a $30,000 balance at Fidelity.
"Like most people, I've got savings in a lot of different places," says 73-year-old Massachusetts resident Lee
Spelke. This year he signed up for the Fidelity account. "The No. 1 thing was to try and pull it all together so I
could make informed decisions," he says.
The financial-services industry is turning its attention to retirees for good reason right now, as older baby
boomers approach the day they get their gold watches. "It all comes back to demographics," says Jeffrey
Carney, president of Fidelity Personal Investments.
Providing investment advice to retirees is nothing new, of course. But these new tools pull together the
lessons learned by investors and planners in recent years. Typically, for instance, the new products start out
by developing a detailed retirement budget: Fidelity's expense worksheets include 36 items ranging from
groceries and Internet bills to supplemental Medicare premiums and long-term-care insurance. Expenses can
be defined to rise or fall in the future -- which, for example, can help individuals factor in a mortgage that's
about to be paid off, or declining travel expenses as they age.
This kind of dynamic budgeting is a step forward from what's been available at other firms, in which such
calculations would have to be done by hand.
Often the products draw on advances in statistical-analysis methods that until recently weren't readily
available to investors, and until now had been focused on helping investors figure out how to save for
retirement. In particular, financial planners are using what is known as the Monte Carlo method, which is a
way of stress-testing a portfolio to determine the odds that it will leave a retiree with enough money years or
decades into the future. (The method was originally given its name by atomic-bomb scientists for the link that
its statistical underpinnings have with games of chance.)
The Monte Carlo method involves running thousands of "what-if" situations on a portfolio using historical
stock- and bond-market returns. The idea: Build a portfolio today that's likely reach its goals in the future 80%
or 90% of the time. "When people see a Monte Carlo simulator and they see a 50-50 chance of going
broke...that gets folks' attention," says Michael Maloon, a financial adviser in San Ramon, Calif.
A key illustration of the Monte Carlo impact can be seen simply by varying withdrawal rates. It wasn't long ago
that the conventional wisdom was that on a portfolio expected to earn 7% over time, an investor could safely
pull out 7% a year and not risk running out of money.
But while that seems logical, it fails to take into account a simple fact: The markets don't move in a straight
line. Experts began questioning the conventional wisdom on withdrawals during the mid-1990s, but during the
bull market, when stocks were rising 20% a year, few heeded the message. That changed when stocks went
into a three-year bear market.
Tom Moulton retired in January 2000 -- three months before the top of the market. As the market declined
and took a chunk out of his retirement savings, he realized it was unlike watching the market's ups and downs
while he was still employed. "It was a whole different thing when it took absolute dollars," he says. "You
suddenly realize there is a finite amount of money."
Experts now couple Monte Carlo simulation with varying withdrawal rates, and the results are startling.
A retiree with a portfolio of 60% stocks and 40% bonds who pulls out 7% a year has only a 30% chance of
making that money last 25 years, according to T. Rowe Price Group Inc. That same retiree withdrawing 4% a
year has a 94% chance of success. In fact, these days, experts say that depending on the account size, 4%
is often the withdrawal rate that works best.
The new methods also routinely take into account more sophisticated models of price inflation. The recent
jump in gas prices aside, overall inflation hasn't been on the radar screen of many investors. But even slight
changes in the inflation rate, over time, can have an amplified effect: At inflation of 3% a year, a retiree's
expenses could double within 25 years, but at a rate of 5%, costs would triple.
Another important variable: health-care costs, which have been rising at more than double the pace of the
overall inflation rate. The planning tools offered by Fidelity and Oppenheimer both factor into their
calculations higher rates of inflation for medical costs.
---
Spending It in Retirement

--- Page 220 ---
Page 220 of 230 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
An array of new investment tools and products are targeting retirees:
Fidelity Investments - Income Management Account
What it does: Combines a detailed budgeting and investment-advice program with real-time monitoring of
whether a retiree is meeting spending and savings goals.
Cost/Availability: The account costs $50 a year, though that can be waived under certain conditions. Current
Fidelity clients also have access to a retirement-income planning tool online.
Vanguard Group - Retirement Center
What it does: This month, several new worksheets and calculators are being added to Vanguard's on-line
retirement center, including ones to help retirees avoid common pitfalls when withdrawing money from
retirement accounts.
Cost/Availability: Free on Vanguard's Web site.
Merrill Lynch &Co. - Retirement Paycheck Service
What it does: The program, launching soon, will help the firm's financial advisers plan for and track spending
and withdrawals from retirees' accounts. The program is coupled with a new tool the firm rolled out late in
2004 that shows whether investors are on track to meet savings goals.
Cost/Availability: Free for Merrill Lynch clients with at least $20,000 accounts

Natural-Gas Futures Heat Up to a Record Close
Commodities Report
Natural-Gas Futures Heat Up to a Record Close
By Maya Jackson Randall and Spencer Jakab Dow Jones Newswires
448 words
29 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In an early taste of what consumers can expect by way of higher heating bills this winter, natural-gas futures
skyrocketed 10% to almost $14 per million British thermal units, its highest closing on record.
The move added weight to a warning from the Natural Gas Supply Association alerting consumers that colder
weather in the East combined with hurricane-related supply disruptions along the Gulf Coast will likely mean
higher natural-gas prices across the country this winter. "Consumers should be prepared to pay higher bills
this winter," said association Chairman Joseph Blount.
Natural-gas futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange for delivery in October soared $1.251 to $13.907
per million BTUs. The expiration of the October contract at the same time that the delivery point for Nymex
futures, Louisiana's Henry Hub, has been closed for a week, sowed uncertainty in the market and added to
yesterday's volatility.
Traders are openly talking about price spikes as high as $20, according to Guy Gleichmann, president of
futures broker United Strategic Investors Group.
The natural-gas association, which represents producers and marketers, said in its annual winter outlook that
Hurricane Katrina-related production losses, an anticipated increase in heating demand, and the potential
effect from Hurricane Rita "will result in upward pressure on the natural-gas market, compared with the
average for last heating season." The weather "is the largest single factor affecting natural-gas demand and
customer bills," the association said. The group highlighted supply constraints in the market and urged
customers to conserve this winter by turning thermostats down a couple of degrees and weatherizing their
homes.
Supply remains a significant concern in the wake of two hurricanes that have "shut in," or closed down,
production that can't be readily replaced with imports. Hurricanes Katrina and Rita have delayed or halted
production of about 180 billion cubic feet, or 4.9% of the yearly gas production in the Gulf of Mexico.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the Nymex rallied sharply, with options-related activity and strength in
energy futures lifting trade. Central-bank selling of gold reserves was less than anticipated, lending support.
September gold rose $7.10 an ounce to $469.70, while most-active December gained $6.90 to $473.10.
COFFEE: Futures on the New York Board of Trade rose 5% on technical-chart and commodity-fund buying.
September coffee rose 4.75 cents a pound to 93.40 cents. Most-active December also rallied 4.75 cents a
pound to 92.90 cents.

Stocks Edge Up But Oil Rebound Holds Down Gain
Monday's Markets
Stocks Edge Up But Oil Rebound Holds Down Gain
By E.S. Browning
499 words
27 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE STOCK MARKET rebounded after its worst weekly decline in three months, but the recovery was small
as oil prices turned up during the day amid fresh worries about Hurricane Rita's impact on energy prices.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished ahead 24.04 points, or 0.23%, at 10443.63. It had been up 89.64
points soon after the opening bell and then went down 9.48 points late in the day before a final rebound. It still
is down 3.2% for 2005.
"It was a day of muted relief, that things weren't worse. But that said, energy prices didn't pull back. There is
definitely a demand-supply concern," said Warren Epstein, head trader at brokerage firm Kabrik Trading.
Gold prices rose and bond prices fell, reflecting concerns about inflation. Michael Moskow, president of the
Chicago Federal Reserve Bank, told reporters that the Fed needs to reduce "accommodation" -- Fedspeak
for raising interest rates to prevent inflation. Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan delivered fresh warnings about
housing speculation and mortgage borrowing, in a speech to bankers, which also made investors nervous.
Stocks began the day with a surge and oil prices slumped, on relief that Rita bypassed the main Gulf oil
refining and production facilities. But then reports from Texas suggested that it could take longer than
expected to bring those refineries that were affected back on line.
Traders concluded that supply and price problems could last longer than they thought and crude-oil futures
finished up $1.63 at $65.82 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Adding to the jitters, President
Bush urged drivers to avoid unnecessary travel.
Retailers, chemical companies and other oil-sensitive stocks, which began with gains, turned down.
Investors initially were pleased at news that existing-home sales in August were the second-highest level
ever, but by day's end, energy prices outweighed most other concerns.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.03%, or 0.34 points, to 1215.63, up a spare 0.3% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.22%, or 4.62 points, to 2121.46, down 2.5% in 2005.
Outside the U.S., stocks gained in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.09%, or 2.01 points, to 186.88.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.55 billion shares traded, 1,838 stocks rose and 1,452 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 14/32, or $4.38 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.302%. The 30-year bond was down 23/32 to yield 4.565%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 112.20 yen, down from 112.42 yen, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2072 from $1.2047.

HURRICANE RITA shut down a huge swath of the U.S.'s energy infrastructure, but the storm's long-term
economic impact may be muted. While the damage appeared limited, it showed how vulnerable the energy
system has become.

car maker.
